DEPARTMENT OF ENVIRONMENTAL ENGINEERING 
Master thesis: Life-cycle assessment of different sludge  
treatment technologies in Denmark with focus on pyrolysis 
 
 
 
  
 
 
Supervisors: Anders Damgaard (DTU), Maria Faragò (DTU) and Per Henrik Nielsen 
(VCS) 
Author: Daniel Krisa | s200371 
Submitted, 2nd February 2022 
==PAGE:2==
Executive Summary 
 
i 
 
Executive Summary 
In accordance with the aim of the Danish government to become carbon neutral by 2050, Dan-
ish water resource and recovery facilities are attempting to take responsibility and reduce 
their greenhouse gas emissions. However, traditional treatment of sewage sludge generally 
inherits the emissions of greenhouse gases in their processes. To tackle this issue a new tech-
nology is currently introduced in Denmark – sewage sludge pyrolysis.  
The goal of this thesis was to make an initial life-cycle assessment of the environmental im-
pacts of sewage sludge pyrolysis and to compare them to impacts from the established tech-
nologies direct field application and incineration. Another goal was to investigate which pro-
cesses were sensitive and uncertain and should therefore be focussed on in future studies. As 
no full-scale pyrolysis plant in Denmark had been built by the time this thesis was conducted, 
impact modelling was mainly based on data from two pilot pyrolysis plants, literature, and 
assumptions. To perform the life-cycle assessment, a mass and energy balance was set-up 
based on data from pilot-plants operated by VandCenter Syd and a technology provider. The 
energy balance was critically evaluated and adapted to the modelled case. To model environ-
mental impacts the life-cycle impact method EF 3.0 was used. 
The main outcome of the study was that pyrolysis had a lower climate change impact than 
direct field application and incineration. Other environmental impacts were regarded as well, 
and mostly favoured pyrolysis. However, due to significant data gaps in the modelling no 
conclusions could be drawn from these impacts. Direct field application led to more avoided 
impacts on the use of metals and minerals. This was because direct field application could 
substitute higher fertiliser amounts than biochar from pyrolysis, as more nutrients could be 
recycled on fields and subsequently be taken up by plants. The thesis revealed that a lower TS 
content led to a worse environmental performance of sewage sludge pyrolysis, while it led to 
an improvement of impacts from direct field application and incineration.  
The thesis revealed that future studies should especially focus on the modelling of flue gas 
emissions and flue gas treatment, the leaching of phosphorus from biochar during field ap-
plication, the substitution of fertiliser from biochar, the wastewater treatment of condensed 
water from the steam dryer and the energy balance of the system. 
 
 
==PAGE:3==
Sammenfatning 
 
ii 
 
Sammenfatning 
I overensstemmelse med den danske regerings målsætning om at blive CO2-neutral i 2050, 
forsøger danske rensningsanlæg at tage ansvar og reducere deres drivhusgasemissioner. Tra-
ditionel behandling af spildevandsslam er dog generelt forbundet med emissioner af drivhus-
gasser. For at løse dette problem introduceres der i øjeblikket en ny teknologi i Danmark: 
pyrolyse af spildevandsslam.  
Målet med dette speciale er at foretage en indledende livscyklusvurdering af miljøpåvirknin-
gerne fra pyrolyse af spildevandsslam og at sammenligne dem med påvirkningerne fra de 
etablerede teknologier - direkte anvendelse af slam på markerne og forbrænding. Et andet 
mål var at undersøge, hvilke processer der var følsomme og usikre og derfor burde fokuseres 
på i fremtidige undersøgelser. Siden der ikke var bygget et fuldskala-pyrolyseanlæg i Dan-
mark på det tidspunkt, hvor denne rapport blev udført, var modelleringen af miljøpåvirknin-
ger hovedsagelig baseret på data fra to pilotpyrolyseanlæg, litteratur og antagelser. For at 
foretage livscyklusvurderingen, blev der opstillet en masse- og energibalance baseret på data 
fra pilotanlæg, som blev drevet af VandCenter Syd og en teknologiudvikler. Energibalancen 
blev kritisk evalueret og tilpasset til den modellerede sag. Livcyklusmetoden EF 3.0 blev an-
vendt til moduleringen af miljøpåvirkninger.  
Hovedresultatet af undersøgelsen var, at pyrolyse har en lavere klimapåvirkning end direkte 
anvendelse på markerne og forbrænding. Andre miljøpåvirkninger blev også undersøgt, og 
de fleste favoriserede pyrolyse. På grund af betydelige datamangler i modelleringen kunne 
der dog ikke drages konklusioner om disse miljøpåvirkninger. Direkte udbringning på mar-
kerne førte til flere undgåede påvirkninger på brugen af metaller og mineraler. Dette skyldes, 
at direkte udbringning på markerne kunne erstatte større mængder gødning end biokul fra 
pyrolyse, da flere næringsstoffer kunne genanvendes på markerne og efterfølgende optages 
af planterne. Afhandlingen viste, at et lavere TS-indhold førte til dårligere miljøpræstationer 
ved pyrolyse af spildevandsslam, mens det førte til en forbedring af påvirkningerne fra di-
rekte anvendelse på markerne og forbrænding.  
Denne afhandling konkluderer, at fremtidige undersøgelser især bør fokusere på modellering 
af røggasemissioner og røggasrensning, udvaskning af fosfor fra biokul når slam bliver gen-
anvendt på markerne, substitution af gødning fra biokul, spildevandsbehandling af kondens-
vand fra damptørreren og energibalancen i systemet. 
==PAGE:4==
Preface 
 
iii 
 
Preface 
This master thesis was created to assess and compare the environmental impacts of different 
treatment technologies of sewage sludge originating from wastewater treatment. The focus of 
the report was a new promising treatment technology called “sludge pyrolysis”. The thesis 
was conducted at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU) under supervision of Maria 
Faragò (DTU), Anders Damgaard (DTU) and Per Henrik Nielsen from VandCenter Syd. Data 
was provided by VandCenter Syd and a technology provider of sewage sludge pyrolysis 
plants. The model, which was developed in this report will be used as a basis for further de-
velopment and improved to evaluate environmental impacts of producing activated carbon 
from sludge pyrolysis. This further work will be undertaken in the MUDP project “Circular 
resource utilization of residual sludge: Development of unit to produce Activated Carbon 
from Biochar” financed by the Danish Environmental Protection Agency under the Danish 
Eco-Innovation program, as well as the VUDP project “Sustainability of three sludge treat-
ment technologies and the use of residues as fertiliser” financed by the Danish Water and 
Wastewater Association DANVA.  
 
 
 
 
Kongens Lyngby, January 2022 
Daniel Krisa 
Master student

==PAGE:17==
Introduction 
 
1 
 
1. Introduction 
In 2019 “climate emergency” was chosen as the word of the year. The word was chosen by the 
Oxford University Press, which each year decides on a word “to reflect ethos, mood, or pre-
occupations of that particular year”. Climate emergency was among others chosen because of 
the “enormous discussion” around climate change in that year, but also because of the “esca-
lation in the language used by people to articulate concerns about climate change” (Oxford 
Languages, 2019). Moreover, the UN-Secretary, António Guterres called climate change the 
“defining issue of our time”(Guterres, 2018). 
This shows how important the topic of climate change is. In the 2015 Paris Agreement, 196 
countries agreed to strive for a global warming of less than 2 degrees Celsius in comparison 
to 1990 (United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (UNFCCC), 2021a). The 
commitment to this goal was underlined at the United Nations Climate Change Conference 
(COP26) in November 2021 by 153 countries introducing new emission targets for 2030 to 
reach it (UNFCCC, 2021b). To give the fight against climate change legitimacy, the Danish 
Parliament implemented the goal of becoming carbon neutral by 2050 into their national leg-
islation (Danish Energy Agency, 2021).  
As with other industrial sectors, the wastewater sector is attempting to reduce their emissions 
to net-zero. Since emissions of nitrous oxide (N2O) – a much more potent greenhouse gas than 
carbon dioxide (CO2) – are intrinsic to the process of treating wastewater treatment, carbon 
negative technologies are needed to save CO2 (Danish Water and Wastewater Association 
(DANVA), 2020). One potentially carbon-negative technology widely discussed in research is 
pyrolysis. Pyrolysis is capable of converting carbon (C) from sewage sludge (SS) into biochar 
(BC), which is an effective storage form of carbon capable of removing carbon from the carbon 
cycle for several 100 years (Danish Envirnmental Protection Agency (Danish EPA), 2020). The 
focus of this thesis is the pyrolysis of SS, as this is a technology fostered by countries in the 
European Union (EU) through increasing restrictions on established sludge treatment tech-
nologies like direct land application and direct landfilling. Pyrolysis seems to be a feasible 
alternative to these technologies (Community Research and Development Information Service 
(CORDIS), 2016). 
SS is produced through treating wastewater. In 2019, 113,000 tons of SS were produced in 
Denmark (Danish EPA, 2020a). Dealing with these amounts of SS is an issue, as it contains 
both heavy metals, organic pollutants and pathogens. However, it is also an opportunity as 
==PAGE:18==
Introduction 
 
2 
 
wastewater contains nutrients and biogenic carbon (European Commission (EC), 2001). These 
might be useful in the agricultural sector, but also - depending on the products formed - in 
other industries. The currently established SS treatment technologies all have some shortcom-
ings, which make researchers strive for new sludge treatment technologies. 
The state-of-the art treatment for SS in Denmark is aerobic or anaerobic sludge stabilisation 
and subsequent sludge dewatering (DANVA, 2009). Thereafter, SS is either directly applied 
on the field as fertiliser, incinerated and then disposed, or composted and after that applied 
on the field (Danish EPA, 2010). The pyrolysis of SS is a relatively old technology newly dis-
covered for SS treatment. Pyrolysis is at least 60 years old and has come into focus because it 
is believed that it might be a feasible sludge treatment technology addressing the shortcom-
ings of the above-named established technologies (Balboa Pacific Corporation’s Technology, 
2021).  
The environmental impacts of state-of-the art SS treatment in Denmark have already been 
investigated by several studies (Sendoh Akwo, 2008; Kirkeby, 2013; Yoshida et al., 2018). How-
ever, the environmental impacts of SS pyrolysis have not yet been thoroughly assessed com-
prehensively. Thomsen (2018) assessed the global warming potential of pyrolysis based on a 
pyrolysis pilot plant in a non peer-reviewed report. However, he did neither consider further 
impact categories nor did he provide an energy and mass balance of the assessed pyrolysis 
plant. 
Mári (2019) performed a life-cycle assessment (LCA) comparing the impacts of different phos-
phorus recovery technologies from SS. Hence, he also modelled the treatment of SS. In his 
analysis he included direct land application, SS incineration, SS pyrolysis and struvite precip-
itation. Mári (2019) did not especially focus on pyrolysis. He modelled the environmental im-
pacts deploying the LCA software SimaPro. The obtained results were only presented as nor-
malised and weighted results, but no characterised results were provided. Additionally, no 
mass or energy balance of the pyrolysis process was shown. This shows that there were only 
a few studies around pyrolysis conducted in Denmark and that there is still a gap in research 
when assessing environmental impacts of SS pyrolysis in Denmark.   
The present study attempts to address the above-mentioned research gaps. Thus, it has the 
objective of comparing the environmental performance of the established SS treatment tech-
nologies, namely direct field application and incineration, with pyrolysis in the case of a small 
==PAGE:19==
Introduction 
 
3 
 
water resource recovery facility (WRRF) under deployment of LCA. To fulfil this objective the 
following sub-goals were defined: 
1. Performance of a mass balance and energy balance based on the harmonisation of pri-
mary data from two different pilot-scale pyrolysis plants extrapolated to match a full-
scale SS pyrolysis plant 
2. Critical assessment of the energy balance based on primary data of a technology pro-
vider 
3. Investigation of critical parameters and processes for the environmental performance 
of SS pyrolysis by combination of perturbation, uncertainty and scenario analysis 
The present study provides additional knowledge by performing an LCA as well as a mass- 
and energy balance of pyrolysis and the two most common SS treatment technologies: direct 
field application and incineration. The focus of this study is on a small WRRF, which is, ac-
cording to CORDIS (2016), exactly the intended user of SS pyrolysis. This is because often new 
solutions for sludge management are rather developed for middle to large WRRFs and hence 
are not economically feasible for smaller plants. However, this is intended to be changed with 
SS pyrolysis (CORDIS, 2016).  
Up to the point of time when this study was conducted, no mass balances and energy balances 
of Danish SS pyrolysis plants depending on the total solids (TS) content of SS were available. 
This is a gap, which the present study aims to fill. Hereby, data from a pilot-plant operated 
by VCS and a pilot-plant operated by a technology provider is combined to build the mass 
and energy balance. The use of the LCA modelling software EASETECH provides the possi-
bility to follow more accurately the flow of mass fractions throughout the process.  
In addition, the study aims for the development of an easily adaptable model in EASETECH, 
which can be quickly adjusted to newly available data. This is important as the first two full-
scale SS pyrolysis plants are built in Odsherred and Søndersø while this study is progress. 
New data from these plants need to be implemented to finally get an environmental assess-
ment of how pyrolysis performs in “real life”, as the model until now is based on assumptions, 
pilot plant and literature data.  
The energy balance has a large influence on the environmental performance of pyrolysis. De-
pending on the energy content in sludge and the efficiency of pyrolysis in utilising this energy, 
additional energy from liquified petroleum gas (LPG) or electricity may be required. This has 
==PAGE:20==
Introduction 
 
4 
 
a significant influence on the environmental emissions. Data provided by a technology pro-
vider is hence critically evaluated with own calculations to provide hints on where to focus 
further research, when considering the energy balance. Finally, this study includes a qualita-
tive uncertainty assessment combined with a quantitative sensitivity analysis and a scenario 
analysis providing a greater robustness of the results.  
 
 
==PAGE:21==
Background 
 
5 
 
2. Background 
This Section is intended to provide an overview about SS treatment technologies which 
were assessed in this study. As the focus of the study lies on pyrolysis, this technology 
is described more in detail with its main products and parameters influencing its perfor-
mance. Furthermore, findings of other LCA studies assessing the environmental perfor-
mance of pyrolysis, incineration and direct application are investigated and compared. 
2.1. 
Direct field application 
The currently most common sludge treatment technology in Denmark used for stabilised 
sludge is direct field application (Danish EPA, 2020a), in the following referred to as “direct 
application”. The reason for its widespread application is its low cost in comparison to other 
technologies, which lie at around 2,000 DKK/ton TS. To recycle SS directly back on the field 
SS needs to comply with the Danish regulation on the use of waste for agricultural purposes 
(BEK No. 1650 from 13th Dec., 2006), which includes the European Sewage Sludge Directive 
(86/278/EEC) (Waste and Resource Network Denmark (DAKOFA), 2021).  
According to the regulation, SS needs to be hygienically stabilised, meaning that pathogens 
are removed or reduced, odour is eliminated, and organic matter is prevented from later de-
composition (Rorat et al., 2019). This can be done by anaerobic digestion, aerobic long-time 
aeration, composting, lime stabilisation or sludge mineralisation. Furthermore, it needs to be 
below the threshold values for heavy metals and organic pollutants (Danish Ministry for 
Environment and Food, 2013). After stabilisation, SS might be stored (depending on local con-
ditions) and is afterwards dewatered, stored again and finally directly applied on the field 
(Larsen et al., 2018). 
In 2019, 78 % of SS in Denmark was applied directly on fields. SS consists among others of 
phosphorus (P), nitrogen (N) and potassium (K) - the three key nutrients for plants, which 
usually are added to the field in form of fertiliser (Lines-Kelly, 1992). As SS contains these 
nutrients, it can replace conventional fertiliser on the field to a certain extend. Hence, if SS is 
applied on fields, less fertiliser is needed, which yields the positive environmental result that 
emissions from industrial fertiliser production are avoided.  
When sludge is applied on the field it leads to nitrous oxide, carbon dioxide and methane 
emissions, which pose an impact on the environment. In this thesis the term “impact” is re-
lated to negative repercussions on the environment, while the term “avoided impact” implies 
==PAGE:22==
Background 
 
6 
 
a positive consequence for the environment. This means that, for instance, the capture and 
storage of CO2 emissions from an incineration plant leads to an “avoided” impact as no CO2 
is emitted, which would otherwise have been released and damaged the environment. 
Whereas the release of CO2 implies an impact and therewith negative consequences for the 
environment.  
In addition, nutrients from SS may be leached to groundwater causing eutrophication (EC, 
2009; Yoshida et al., 2018). Heavy metals and organic pollutants, which are contained in 
wastewater and subsequently in SS as well, pose a threat to the environment. There is a dis-
cussion ongoing on whether these heavy metal concentrations have a negative effect on the 
environment at all (Toft Ingvertsen et al., 2016). However, out of a precautionary principle, 
potential pollutant emissions should rather be avoided if possible (EC, 2000). 
2.2. 
Incineration 
To minimise the impact of heavy metals on the environment, another technology has been 
developed which prevents the application of SS and therewith heavy metals on the field: SS 
incineration. This technology is referred to as “incineration” throughout the report. In 2019, 
14 % of SS in Denmark was treated by incineration (Danish EPA, 2020a). For incineration, SS 
is either directly burned or pre-dried before incineration and subsequently burned. For SS to 
be self-burning – meaning it can be incinerated without pre-treatment as its own energy con-
tent is sufficient for evaporating its water content - a TS content of 28 - 34% is required. How-
ever, it can also be pre-dried with the energy coming from the following incineration itself. 
Then, TS contents down to 23% are sufficient for incineration. The produced energy from in-
cineration can be used to provide heat for the local grid. The remaining sewage sludge ash 
(SSA) - containing high contents of P and heavy metals – is currently landfilled (DANVA, 
2009, 2010). The flue gas meanwhile needs to be cleaned from pollutants such as NOx, SO2, 
particulate matter (PM), heavy metals and polychlorinated dioxins as well as furans (Liang et 
al., 2021). 
Unlike in direct application, no methane or nitrous oxide emissions are produced during in-
cineration. Nor are there any heavy metals or organic pollutants applied on the field as SSA 
produced during incineration is landfilled. Generally, incineration is the most expensive 
sludge treatment technology in Denmark with around 5,500 DKK/ton TS. However, there are 
scaling advantages depending on the size of the incineration plant. Hence, incineration plants 
can be advantageous, if fostered by local conditions (Kirkeby, 2013). Further disadvantages of 
==PAGE:23==
Background 
 
7 
 
sludge incineration are the loss of nutrients, the chemical-consuming flue gas treatment, and 
the need to uphold landfill capacities in order to landfill future produced SSA (DANVA, 2009). 
As the space for landfills in Denmark is limited, Denmark’s strategy is to minimize the amount 
of waste that is landfilled. Using SS incineration is against this strategy and needs to stop at 
one point in the future due to the mentioned space limitation (Hjelmar, 1996; Danish EPA, 
2021). 
2.3. 
Pyrolysis 
As the focus of the present thesis lies in modelling the environmental impacts of treating SS 
with pyrolysis, this this Section will focus more in detail on the pyrolysis process itself and 
the main products produced when pyrolyzing SS. Furthermore, the most important parame-
ters influencing pyrolysis are described shortly. 
2.3.1. 
General process description 
Sewage sludge pyrolysis - from here on referred to as “SS pyrolysis” - is a technology attempt-
ing to tackle both disadvantages from direct application and incineration. Hence, SS pyrolysis 
should minimize emissions from direct application, while at the same time allowing for the 
recycling of nutrients in SS (Thomsen, 2018). Additionally, pyrolysis bears the potential of 
storing carbon in the soil for a period of 500 to 1000 years and could therewith contribute 
positively to tackle climate change (Agriculture & Food LLC, 2018).  
In general, pyrolysis can be described as the thermal decomposition of organic matter due to 
heating in the absence of oxygen (Djandja et al., 2020; U.S. Department of Agriculture, 2021). 
As there are not enough oxygen atoms from sludge for the full oxidation of carbon to CO2 
available, heating forces the long carbon chains to rearrange into smaller ones. The main pro-
cess occurs at a temperature range of 300 to 650 °C during which organic matter is broken 
down and rearranged into the three fractions: synthesis gas also called “syngas”, tar also 
known as pyrolysis oil or bio-oil and in this study referred to as “py oil”, and biochar (BC). 
These fractions are also the three main products of pyrolysis (Djandja et al., 2020).  
Even though pyrolysis technically starts at 300 °C, a pyrolysis reactor consists of the following 
three reaction zones: <200 °C, 200 – 600 °C and >600 °C (Djandja et al., 2020). The exact tem-
perature defining the zones differ depending on the source consulted (Park, Kang and Kim, 
2008; Gao et al., 2014; Kan, Strezov and Evans, 2016; Djandja et al., 2020). In the 1st zone (tem-
perature <200 °C) no typical pyrolysis reactions occur. The residue water, which could not be 
==PAGE:24==
Background 
 
8 
 
dried in the drying process proceeding pyrolysis is evaporated in this zone. Furthermore, 
bonds of hydrated compounds are broken down (Djandja et al., 2020).  
Typical pyrolysis reactions occur in the 2nd zone (200 – 600 °C). With increasing temperature, 
depolymerisation of biodegradable organic matter occurs, but also devolatilization leading to 
py oil. At higher temperatures – above 380 °C - secondary reactions like dehydrogenation, 
decarbonylation, decarboxylation, deoxygenation start cracking the carbon chains of py oil 
and therewith produce syngas.  
In the 3rd zone (>600 °C) further decomposition of py oil as well as inorganic matter occurs 
(Djandja et al., 2020). However, not every pyrolysis process performs all of the above-de-
scribed steps. The process is adapted to the desired outcome. In Appendix B, it is described 
more in detail, which parameters mostly influence pyrolysis and how the process can be ma-
nipulated. 
2.3.2. 
Pyrolysis products 
As mentioned in the previous Section pyrolysis has the three main products: syngas, py oil 
and BC. There is also the possibility of producing activated carbon (AC) by SS pyrolysis. This 
is only qualitatively investigated in Appendix B as no primary data on the production of AC 
became available from the technology provider. However, the production of AC instead of 
BC might yield an improvement of the environmental performance of pyrolysis. This is be-
cause European AC is typically produced on the basis of hard coal - a depletable resource, 
whose exhaustion could be avoided if SS or BC could be utilised for AC production instead.  
However, at first, py oil shall be closer scrutinized. In general, py oil can be described as a 
complex mixture of 300 to 400 compounds, containing water, organic matter and additional 
components depending on the composition of SS in the input (Zaman et al., 2017; Djandja et 
al., 2020). Under these additional components, some harmful compounds like organonitrogen 
species are included (Djandja et al., 2020). According to Fonts et al. (2012) condensed water is 
the compound occurring in highest concentrations in py oil from SS pyrolysis, with contents 
between 20 to 70 % of the total wet weight (%ww).  
Keeping the water content low is essential as it has negative repercussions on the chemical 
stability of the produced oil as well as its lower heating value (LHV) (Fonts et al., 2012).  Py oil 
carries a higher heating value ranging between 22 to 40 MJ/kg, which is according to Djandja 
et al. (2020) lower than the higher heating value of usual conventional liquid oil of 45 to 46 
MJ/kg.  Thus, py oil will usually have a lower higher heating value than conventional oil and 
==PAGE:25==
Background 
 
9 
 
if intended to substitute conventional oil, there is always the need for an upgrading process 
to remove harmful compounds and reduce the water content (Fonts et al., 2012; Djandja et al., 
2020). 
Syngas is a gaseous mixture of CO2, CO, H2, CH4, C2 – C6 hydrocarbons, nitrogen and sulfur 
species. The main components with wet weight shares above 5 ww% are CO2, CO, H2 and CH4 
(Moško et al., 2020). Syngas has an energy content varying between 5 to 36 MJ/kg (Fonts et al., 
2012). The amount of syngas produced and its composition is highly temperature dependant 
(Djandja et al., 2020). Syngas can be used as an energy source and directly combusted to pro-
vide the energy required to run the pyrolysis process and the preceding sludge drying (Fonts 
et al., 2012). Otherwise, syngas can be upgraded to replace natural gas, or it can be used to 
synthesize chemicals or oil (Djandja et al., 2020). 
BC is the third and - if syngas and py oil are burned for energy provision – only product of 
pyrolysis. Zaman et al. (2017) describes BC as a solid carbonaceous material. To depict BC 
more visually: BC is a solid, black, granular mixture of carbon and ash possessing a variety of 
positive properties for soil amendment. Therefore, BC has been subject of ongoing research. 
BC amendment has the potential to increase the electric conductivity , cation-exchange capac-
ity, the amount of nutrients in the soil, soil organic matter, microbial activity, pH, porosity 
and water holding capacity (Glaser et al., 2015; Koul and Taak, 2018; Rawat, Saxena and 
Sanwal, 2019; Raave et al., 2020). 
Furthermore, there are studies indicating that BC application on soil leads to reduced emis-
sions to the environment from greenhouse gases, heavy metals, nutrients and organic pollu-
tants due to an increased adsorptive capacity of the amended soil (Rawat, Saxena and Sanwal, 
2019; Raave et al., 2020). According to Koul and Taak (2018), BC is used for the remediation of 
soils contaminated with heavy metals or organic pollutants.  
In contrast, Glaser et al. (2015) state that there are studies where biochar amendment did not 
show any enhanced soil properties. From most literature reviewed for this study, it seems, 
however, that BC amendment has positive consequences on soil properties in most cases, alt-
hough the extent of the effect is strongly dependent on the local conditions of each case. Re-
garding the improvement of greenhouse gas emissions from the field due to BC amendment, 
Raave et al. (2020) and Gao et al. (2020) indicate that this is a rather disputed result in scientific 
literature and is not satisfactorily proven. However, in contrast to SS, BC does not lead to 
direct emissions of nitrous oxide and methane when applied on the field. 
==PAGE:26==
Background 
 
10 
 
Another argued topic is whether the uptake of nutrients into plants increases or decreases due 
to BC amendment. Here, Gao et al. (2020) showed that when regarding N uptake, different 
studies yield different results ranging from an elevated to a lowered N uptake by plants. 
Raave et al. (2020) found a significantly increased uptake of P and K by plants from hay-de-
rived biochar. Therefore, and because N uptake by plants is not satisfactorily verified, it as-
sumed in this study that only P and K is taken up by plants.  
In comparison to SS, very small heavy metal fractions are leached out from BC on a SS basis 
when applied on the field. Organic pollutants can be eliminated during the pyrolysis process 
when sufficient temperatures are reached (Saud et al., 2021). As BC is a useful product, it is 
not landfilled as the ash from incineration and therewith landfill capacity is saved. Finally, 
pyrolysis might allow the provision of heat from the process if the SS has a sufficient energy 
content. Therefore, pyrolysis could be able to address the shortcomings of both direct appli-
cation and incineration. 
Depending on the desired goal, which is attempted to be achieved by pyrolysis, the amount 
of the three main products can be shifted by adjusting process parameters of pyrolysis. Table 
1 shows the effect of increasing different process parameters on the amount of the three main 
products. In the following this is only discussed briefly for the most relevant parameters for 
the thesis. A more comprehensive discussion also of the effect of parameters on the composi-
tion of the three main products can be found in Appendix B. 
Table 1: Effect of increasing different process parameters on the amount of main product from pyrolysis

==PAGE:27==
Background 
 
11 
 
According to Djandja et al. (2020), temperature is the most important factor for pyrolysis. A 
surging temperature leads to a decrease in BC and to an increase in syngas yields. The 
amount of py oil increases up to a certain temperature and decreases thereafter  (Zhang et 
al., 2021). An interesting feature, which is important for optimizing the energy balance, is 
that a rise in temperature also yields an upsurge in the energy content of syngas (Tomasi 
Morgano et al., 2018). Thus, by increasing temperature not only can more syngas be ob-
tained, but the syngas contains a higher energy content, which can be made available when 
burning syngas. Hence, temperature is an important factor when optimizing the energy bal-
ance. 
Heating rate and residence time are often mentioned together, as a high heating rate combined 
with a low residence time is usually necessary when striving for an optimization of the py oil 
yield (Fonts et al., 2012; Djandja et al., 2020). Ash and VS content in the input SS to the pyrolysis 
affect the amount of syngas and BC produced. Hereby, higher VS contents foster the produc-
tion of syngas, while ash improves BC production (Chanaka Udayanga et al., 2019; Djandja et 
al., 2020). Generally, the characteristics of the SS input are important for the pyrolysis process, 
as for instance the amount of energy carrying components defines whether enough energy for 
running the process is available or whether additional energy is needed for running the pro-
cess. 
2.4. 
Comparison of SS treatment technologies summarised 
The advantages and disadvantages of the three SS treatment technologies, which were evalu-
ated in the previous Sections, are summarized in Table 2. The green colour indicates a positive, 
while the orange colour indicates a negative consequence for environment or economy. Just 
from looking at the table it seems that pyrolysis outperforms the other technologies as there 
are more positive and less negative consequences than from the other technologies. However, 
it should be noted that this is only a selection of parameters and that an LCA is required to 
truly evaluate the impacts on the environment of the SS treatment technologies, which is at-
tempted in this study. In the next Section, it is investigated which LCA studies have already 
been conducted regarding SS treatment technologies.

2.5. 
Literature review of life-cycle assessments focussing on  
sewage sludge treatment 
In this Section, results and findings from previous LCA studies concerning the three SS treat-
ment technologies are compared and assessed. As the focus of this study lies on SS pyrolysis 
in particular, only studies that include SS pyrolysis in their assessment are regarded. A short 
summary with all studies detected including their main outcome can be found in the end of 
this Section (Table 3). 
From the studies reviewed, 9 studies included a comparative LCA of SS pyrolysis with other 
SS treatment technologies. In 7 of these studies, the final result was that pyrolysis had a better 
environmental performance than other technologies. These outcomes were mainly obtained 
either by weighting different impact categories and thereby receiving one aggregated result, 
or by regarding those impact categories specifically affected by SS treatment. 
The system boundaries differed from study to study, which is the reason why it is difficult to 
compare exact results with each other. Most studies started either directly with SS from 
wastewater treatment, or with thickened and dewatered SS (TDSS) and ended with the appli-
cation of BC on fields. There were different life-cycle impact assessment (LCIA) methodolo-
gies deployed in the studies and no clear trend could be found as to which LCIA methodology 
is most widely applied. Only one study used the same methodology as in this study – EF 3.0, 
==PAGE:29==
Background 
 
13 
 
which is recommended to be used by the International Life Cycle Data system (ILCD) (Sphera, 
2022). 
In the following, the outcomes of some of the above-mentioned studies are presented. The 
only studies considering direct field application were undertaken by Thomsen (2018), Mári 
(2019), and Mayer, Bhandari and Gäth (2021). Thomsen, (2018) and Mári (2019) both found a 
better case for pyrolysis than for direct application. However, Mayer, Bhandari and Gäth 
(2021) found that direct application displayed the best environmental performance and pyrol-
ysis the worst. They analysed hydrothermal carbonisation combined with incineration, pure 
incineration, direct application, and pyrolysis combined with an incineration of BC. Except 
for direct application, all systems deployed a P recovery, where P is chemically extracted from 
the ashes.  
According to Mayer, Bhandari and Gäth (2021) a factor adding specifically to the environmen-
tal impact of pyrolysis was the need for additional N-containing fuel to provide enough en-
ergy for the process. When incinerated, about 1 % of N in the fuel was converted to N2O, thus 
impacting Climate Change (CC), while further N in the fuel added to “Particulate Matter For-
mation” (PM) (Mayer, Bhandari and Gäth, 2021).  
The setup deployed by Mayer, Bhandari and Gäth (2021) is different from the other studies as 
BC from pyrolysis was subsequently incinerated and finally P was recovered under deploy-
ment of chemicals. In most of the other studies BC was directly applied on fields without P 
recovery. Barry et al. (2019) deployed BC from pyrolysis as coal substitute in in a cement kiln 
and the ash from the kiln as cement filler. This scenario showed the best environmental per-
formance in his study followed by pyrolysis with BC applied on fields and incineration with 
and without energy recovery.  
Gievers, Loewen and Nelles (2021) investigated the effect of different BC applications on the 
environmental performance of SS pyrolysis. They reached the conclusion that BC substituting 
peat moss in horticulture had the lowest impact on CC followed by lignite substitution in a 
power plant. This, with the results from Barry et al. (2019), show that the substitution of BC 
can have a high influence on the performance of SS pyrolysis. When comparing the environ-
mental performance of pyrolysis and incineration Gievers, Loewen and Nelles (2021), Wang 
et al. (2013), Nakakubo, Tokai and Ohno (2012) and Mills et al. (2014) all concluded that pyrol-
ysis has a lower environmental impact than incineration.  
==PAGE:30==
Background 
 
14 
 
Li and Feng (2018) found that sole anaerobic digestion had lower impacts on the environment 
than pyrolysis. However, both Li and Feng (2018) and Cao and Pawłowski (2013) detected a 
better environmental performance of pyrolysis combined with anaerobic digestion than sole 
pyrolysis. Nakakubo, Tokai and Ohno (2012) observed that combined treatment of SS together 
with food waste improved the environmental performance of all SS treatment options as-
sessed. A summary of results of the studies found assessing the environmental performance 
of SS pyrolysis and other SS treatment technologies can be viewed in Table 3.

3. Materials and methods 
This Section includes background information for the modelling of the conducted LCA. The 
LCA was performed following the ISO 14040, ISO 14044 standard and the ILCD guidelines 
(EC-JRC, 2010; Hauschild, Rosenbaum and Olsen, 2015). In the following Sections, the most 
important points of goal and scope of the study, the system, system boundaries and the mod-
elling framework are summarised. The full goal and scope definition can be found in Appen-
dix A. 
3.1. 
Goal, scope and functional unit 
As mentioned in Section 1, the present study was performed in order to assess and compare 
environmental impacts of the two most widely applied SS treatment technologies in Denmark 
with the newly upcoming technology SS pyrolysis. The study was conducted to provide a first 
decision support for operators of small WRRFs wishing to improve the environmental perfor-
mance of their operations by deployment of SS pyrolysis. Thus, the focus of this study lied 
especially on SS pyrolysis and the goal was to set-up a comprehensive model for assessing 
environmental impacts in dependence of available data.  
However, as no data from operating full-scale SS pyrolysis plants was available at this point 
of time, the model utilised for assessing environmental impacts was based on data from pilot 
plants, literature and assumptions. The intention with the developed model was to identify 
hot spots of high emissions and to combine these with a sensitivity and uncertainty analysis 
to evaluate, where better data is needed to accurately mirror emissions to the environment. 
Where data was uncertain and sensitive the effect of varying data is investigated with a sce-
nario analysis. The model should also align with the requirement of being easily adaptable to 
new data. Hence, new data from the first full-scale plants might be accommodated easily into 
the model once it has become available. 
For an LCA, it is important to ensure a fair comparison of the assessed services or products. 
Thus, a common basis of comparison needs to be established for each LCA – the functional 
unit (FU). The FU includes the main function of a product or service, its reference flow, ap-
plied timeframe, number of applications, quality and geography, where the service is pro-
vided. Not all the above-mentioned categories are required to be included in the FU, only 
those necessary to ensure an equal comparison (Hauschild, Rosenbaum and Olsen, 2015). The 
==PAGE:35==
Materials and methods 
19 
 
FU for this study was defined as: “Disposal of one ton wet weight of dewatered & thickened 
sewage sludge of a plant specific composition in Denmark for a period of 100 years“.  
Thereby the “disposal of sewage sludge” in alignment with Danish regulations was the main 
function demanded from the assessed SS treatment technologies (Danish Ministry for 
Environment and Food, 2013). The amount was based on the wet weight rather than the TS 
content, as the amount of water to be evaporated has an influence on the energy balance of 
the system. The water content impacts the amount of energy required to be added to the sys-
tem in form of liquid-petroleum gas (LPG), and therefore the needs to be included. TDSS was 
used as an input instead of activated sludge to simplify the system and only compare those 
parts of the system which were expected to be significantly different. The timeframe of 100 
years applied to emissions to the environment, whose impacts are normalised to the impact 
of a specific reference substance over 100 years in each impact category. This ensured compa-
rability of different scenarios in the same impact category. 
3.2. 
Wastewater treatment in Søndersø 
To model environmental impacts of different SS treatment options the SS treatment of one 
specific WRRF was chosen as exemplary case for Denmark. The plant is situated in Søndersø 
and run by VCS. It is a plant treating wastewater for 20,000 person equivalents (PEs), where 
1 PE corresponds to the average wastewater load produced by one person (Henze, 2012).  
The wastewater process is depicted in Figure 1. The wastewater line is visible in blue, while 
the sludge line is shown in grey. The process is shortly explained in the following. To 
wastewater arriving at Søndersø, iron(III) sulfate is added in order to precipitate phosphorus 
compounds. Thereafter, wastewater is cleaned from larger particles by a grit. The subsequent 
treatment step is a sand- and fat trap, where particles are sedimented and fat is floating on the 
surface. Then, wastewater enters the aeration basins – the biological treatment step. In these 
basins, wastewater is aerated under deployment of rotary brush aerators. The aeration is am-
monia driven. Hence, if the ammonia concentration falls below a certain threshold value aer-
ators are turned off and denitrification starts until the ammonia concentration again exceeds 
a certain threshold. After the biological step the now cleaned wastewater enters a clarification, 
where SS can settle. Finally, the water is aerated with oxygen before it is discharged into a 
river (N Almind-Jørgensen 2021, personal communication, 19 Oktober).   
==PAGE:36==
Materials and methods 
20 
 
 
Figure 1: Flow chart wastewater treatment Søndersø (N Almind-Jørgensen 2021, personal communication, 13 Oktober) 
During the treatment of wastewater in the biological step, activated SS is produced. Currently, 
this SS is stabilised during wastewater treatment through long-time aeration. This means that 
SS needs to have an average retention time of at least 14 days in the wastewater treatment. 
Thus, some of the SS is recycled back into the aeration to fulfil this requirement. After the 14 
days, SS is stored for a short time in a tank, thickened by addition of polymers and subse-
quently dewatered (N Almind-Jørgensen 2021, personal communication, 19 Oktober). This is 
where this LCA starts – with TDSS, marked by a red star in the flow chart. After dewatering 
TDSS is stored and subsequently transported and applied to the field.  
With the current dewatering press, the TS content varies between 13 and 19 %. However, as a 
new press is planned to be implemented when the full-scale pyrolysis plant goes into opera-
tion, TDSS with TS contents between 22 and 24 % was assumed as input for the three SS treat-
ment technologies. For the baseline scenario a TS content of 24 % in the input TDSS was used. 
3.3. 
System description and system boundaries 
To perform an LCA, it is important to understand the system, which is modelled thoroughly 
in order to be able to decide where to effectively focus research and allocate resources. Hence, 
in the following Sections the three evaluated sludge treatment technologies are explained 
more thoroughly.  
Furthermore, the system boundaries for each system are described. The system boundaries 
include all processes necessary to sufficiently model the environmental emissions of a system. 
==PAGE:37==
Materials and methods 
21 
 
The system is divided into foreground and background system. The foreground system is the 
part of the system which is specific to it. It can often directly be influenced by technology 
operators and hence, ideally primary data shall be used for modelling (Hauschild, Rosenbaum 
and Olsen, 2015).  
The background system includes the part of the system which is still within the system bound-
aries, but is not specific to it. It can typically not be altered by the technology operator and 
hence Life-cycle inventory (LCI) data from LCI databases is often deployed for modelling 
(Hauschild, Rosenbaum and Olsen, 2015). However, the assumption for this study is that the 
background system can be influenced as well by the technology operators. The reasoning be-
hind this assumption and its implications on the modelling framework are described in Sec-
tion 3.4. 
The following Sections combine the description of the three SS treatment options with the 
delineation of system boundaries, fore- and background system as well as an explanation of 
modelling choices and data sources. 
3.3.1. 
Direct field application 
As hinted in Section 2.1 and 3.2, SS always needs to be stabilised before it can be applied to 
the field. Impacts from SS stabilisation were not subject of investigation in this study, as they 
would have included the modelling of upstream processes, which were outside the scope of 
this study. The assumption in this study was hence, that upstream emissions before sludge 
treatment were the same for the three considered sludge treatment options.  
Direct application is the current technology applied in Søndersø. The modelling of direct ap-
plication is illustrated in Figure 2. Processes are grouped by their colouring into input (green), 
SS line (grey) and emissions to air (blue). The same applies to Figure 3 and Figure 4 in the 
following Sections.
 
Figure 2: Flow chart of direct field application scenario (in red system boundaries, in green boundaries of the foreground 
system) 
Colour code: green: input; blue: emissions to air; grey: SS line 
==PAGE:38==
Materials and methods 
22 
 
The process started at first with TDSS, which was stored in a tank for an average time of half 
a year. This assumption was based on Larsen et al. (2018), who assumed in their study that 
TDSS was stored over the course of a year and then subsequently transported to the field once 
a year. The assumption was also viewed to be reasonable by VCS (N Almind-Jørgensen 2021, 
personal communication, 19 Oktober). Emissions during storage of nitrous oxide, methane 
and CO2 were based on Larsen et al. (2018), as well. 
TDSS is transported to the field. The transport distance was in collaboration with VCS esti-
mated to be 30 km by a EURO 6 standard lorry with a carrying capacity of 16-32 tons (N 
Almind-Jørgensen 2021, personal communication, 19 Oktober). Emissions from transport 
were modelled deploying the Ecoinvent v3.8 database (Ecoinvent, 2021). When TDSS is ap-
plied on the field, it emits nitrous oxides and methane to the air due to anaerobic degradation 
processes. Furthermore, leaching of P, N and heavy metals occurs. The modelling of the emis-
sions of these fractions was based on Yoshida et al. (2018), Phoungthong et al. (2018), Agrafioti 
et al. (2013). However, the modelling is quite uncertain, especially the modelling of heavy 
metal emissions. Here it was assumed that all leaching emissions were directly emitted into 
groundwater. For non-available heavy metal emissions, a leaching value based on the average 
leaching fraction of the other available heavy metals was assumed. 
TDSS applied on the field was used to replace N, P and K fertiliser, which was also considered 
in the present study. Following Yoshida et al. (2018) N in TDSS substituted 45 % conventional 
N fertiliser, P 79 % P fertiliser and the amount of K substituted 100 % conventional K fertiliser. 
95 % of the carbon stored in the char was assumed to be emitted to air (Yoshida et al., 2018). 
The exact sources for modelling of each fraction in the sludge can be viewed in Appendix F.   
3.3.2. 
Incineration 
To model the emissions of the incineration of SS, the model of Faragò et al. (2021) was adapted 
to the case in Søndersø. This meant in practice that the incineration model of Faragò et al. 
(2021) was run with the TDSS composition of Søndersø. It was assumed that fractions in 
Søndersø‘s TDSS transferred in the same way throughout the incineration process as the an-
aerobically digested, thickened and dewatered sewage sludge (ATDSS) originally applied in 
the  model of Faragò et al. (2021). Figure 3 displays the flowchart of SS in the incineration 
process. A similar colour code as for Figure 2 was applied with the addition of orange for heat 
and energy, blue for the gas line and turquoise to indicate several inputs.  
==PAGE:39==
Materials and methods 
23 
 
 
Figure 3: Flow chart of incineration scenario (in red system boundaries, in green boundaries of the foreground system) 
Colour code: green: input; blue: gas line; grey: SS line; orange: heat & electricity; turquoise: several inputs; punctured line: 
heat & electricity transferred 
In the case applied in this study, TDSS was at first dried and subsequently incinerated. Gases 
from drying and incineration were treated together in a flue gas treatment including an elec-
trostatic precipitator for particle removal, chemical dosing and a wet scrubber (Faragò et al., 
2021). The remaining ashes were disposed in an onsite landfill. Heat produced during incin-
eration was partly re-used for drying, partly for supplying heat for district heating.  
Faragò et al. (2021) mainly used primary data for their modelling from a WRRF in Denmark. 
The WRRF mainly treated municipal wastewater corresponding to 400,000 PEs. The compo-
sition of flue gas was based on measurements of the investigated plant and were specific to 
the composition of ATDSS in the input. Thus, the flue gas composition was not modelled in 
dependence of the characteristics of its SS input. This was not adjusted when adapting the 
model to Søndersø‘s case because of simplicity reasons. However, it is discussed later in Sec-
tion 4.3.2. The electricity and heat mix was adjusted to match the modelling framework of this 
study, which is further explained in Section 3.4. The modelling of the energy mix is explained 
in Appendix D. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:40==
Materials and methods 
24 
 
3.3.3. 
SS pyrolysis 
Pyrolysis is, on the one hand, the scenario where most of the of the thesis was focussed, but 
is, on the other hand, the scenario where data was most uncertain. Figure 4 exhibits the flow 
chart of the pyrolysis scenario. The same colour code as for Figure 3 was utilised with the 
addition of wastewater treatment processes coloured in yellow. 
 
Figure 4: Flow chart of pyrolysis scenario (in red system boundaries, in green boundaries of the foreground system) 
Colour code: green: input; blue: gas line; grey: SS line; yellow: wastewater treatment; orange: heat & electricity; punctured 
line: heat & electricity transferred 
The SS input into the pyrolysis plant was first dried with the aid of a steam drier. The water 
evaporated in the process condensed with the aid of cold water. This process step is not shown 
in Figure 4 in order to simplify the figure. The now mixed condensed water could ideally be 
used to provide heat for district heating. However, in this study it was assumed that only the 
heat from cooling the water down 20 K could be utilised for heat generation. As the mixed 
water contained N and C based on the mass balance of data from Thomsen (2018), it needed 
to re-enter wastewater treatment. Thereby, indirect emissions from energy consumption and 
direct emissions specific to wastewater treatment were caused. These emissions were mod-
elled based on own calculations depending on the N content of condensed water evaporated 
from the steam dryer. Calculations included the energy consumption for ammonia oxidation 
to nitrate and emissions of nitrous oxide based on Danish EPA, (2020b). Oxidation of C was 
excluded to simplify the calculations.  
The other output of the steam dryer – dried SS - which now had a TS content of around 90 %, 
entered the pyrolysis reactor. Here, dried SS was pyrolyzed for roughly 30 minutes at 650 °C 
producing the three outputs: BC, syngas and py oil. The fates of the BC and syngas will be 
described here. Py oil was excluded because it was found that only insignificant amounts were 
==PAGE:41==
Materials and methods 
25 
 
produced. A possible explanation for these low non-significant amounts of py oil could be 
that the process was run at a comparatively high temperature and the retention time was ra-
ther long. These two conditions allowed secondary reactions breaking down py oil carbon 
chains into syngas to occur. Thus, very low amounts of py oil were produced, subsequently 
omitted in the modelling. 
BC produced by pyrolysis was stored for an average period of half a year, similar to SS in the 
direct application scenario. Following Thomsen (2018), no emissions during BC storage were 
assumed. It was hence not depicted in Figure 4 to increase readability.  
After storage, BC was transported to the field as in the direct application scenario by a EURO 
6 standard lorry with a carrying capacity of 16-32 tons. BC was subsequently applied on the 
field and replaced P fertiliser. The amount of P fertiliser substituted by BC was according to 
Raave et al. (2020) set to 50 % in the baseline scenario. According to Gao et al. (2020), it could 
not with certainty be concluded whether BC increased or decreased the uptake of N into 
plants. Therefore, no uptake and therewith replacement of fertiliser was assumed in this the-
sis. It was estimated that 10 % of the carbon in the BC was degraded during field application. 
However, 90 % remained stable in the soil and was sequestered (Thomsen, 2018). Leaching of 
heavy metals from BC was presumed to be low and the leaching fractions based on Agrafioti 
et al. (2013) or assumptions can be reviewed in Appendix F. As in Thomsen (2018), no leaching 
or emissions to air from nutrients in BC were modelled.  
The syngas from pyrolysis entered thereafter an incineration step. It was incinerated and sub-
sequently passed two heat exchangers, which were again not visible in Figure 4 to simplify 
the flow chart. The first heat exchanger provided heat for pyrolysis, while the second heat 
exchanger transferred heat to evaporate water in the steam dryer. Depending on the energy 
content of the input SS energy in form of LPG needed to be added to incineration.  
After incineration, the flue gas was treated with a wet scrubber. As there was no data available 
on the composition of syn-, flue gas or the composition of the flue gas after passing the wet 
scrubber some assumptions were made in order to model flue gas emissions. Through a liter-
ature review only a flue gas composition of a Chinese pyrolysis plant was found (Liu et al., 
2018). Other sources detected either had several flue gas treatment steps installed at their 
plants and did not report the flue gas composition before flue gas treatment or used an input, 
which was not SS (Baggio et al., 2008; Seggiani et al., 2012; Chen et al., 2017; Dong et al., 2018). 
The flue gas composition of Liu et al., (2018) was adopted for the present case independent of 
the SS input to decrease complexity. It was assumed that the provided concentrations were 
==PAGE:42==
Materials and methods 
26 
 
given at a dry flue gas oxygen content of 11 % and in mg/Nm³ at a reference temperature of 
0 °C and 1 atm atmospheric pressure.  
The scrubber used for cleaning was a venturi scrubber. According to expert judgement, it was 
eventually possible to meet the emission threshold values to air in the Danish incineration 
regulation set by the Danish ministry for food and environment with the modelled set-up (T 
Hulgaard (2021), personal communication, 29 November; Danish Ministry for Environment 
and Food, 2003). Hence, concentrations of chemical compounds in the flue gas were assumed 
to exactly meet emission thresholds with exception for concentrations, which already before 
had been below the threshold. The water used for flue gas cleaning needed to be treated in 
another wastewater treatment step. In the baseline scenario the average wastewater treatment 
process from Ecoinvent v3.8 was used for modelling emissions of treating this water.  
Mass balance data was retrieved from Thomsen (2018), who mainly used data from a 100-kW 
pilot SS pyrolysis plant operated by VCS, which was only operated in the timeframe of a few 
days. The pilot scale plant was fed with ATDSS from Ejby Mølle (Thomsen, 2018). In this thesis 
the mass balance of Thomsen (2018) was adapted to the SS composition of Søndersø under 
the assumption that the same transfer coefficients could be applied as in Thomsen (2018). As 
the goal of this report was to provide a decision support for VCS on the question, whether it 
is meaningful from an environmental perspective to implement a full-scale pyrolysis plant, 
the mass balance was scaled to a 350-kW full-scale pyrolysis plant.  
Data on the energy balance originated from a technology provider and was based on results 
of a different 100-kW pilot SS pyrolysis plant than the one used by Thomsen (2018). Moreover, 
a different input SS was used than the one used by Thomsen (2018). The energy balance was 
extrapolated by the technology provider to match the case of a 350-kW full-scale plant. This 
energy balance was adapted to the SS composition of Søndersø assuming the same efficiencies 
and transfer coefficients as in the model of the technology provider. The energy content of the 
input TDSS and the output BC was calculated with the Schwanecke formula (see equation 
(1)): 
 
𝐿𝐻𝑉= 348 𝐶% + 939 𝐻% + 105 𝑆% + 63𝑁% −108 𝑂% −24.5 𝐻2𝑂 
(1) 
 
The overall electricity consumption for the whole pyrolysis process was modelled based on 
Thomsen (2018).  
==PAGE:43==
Materials and methods 
27 
 
3.4. 
Modelling framework 
As described in the preface, the study forms a basis for the VUDP project – a known project 
assessing the environmental impacts of SS treatment technologies in Denmark. Thus, it was 
assumed that the study is able to influence large-scale decisions. This is because several WRRF 
operators might read the results of the study and act according to its results. Hence, the mod-
elled scenario considered a roll-out of the SS pyrolysis over all of Denmark. This implied a 
decision context B, which had the following implications on the modelling framework 
(Hauschild, Rosenbaum and Olsen, 2015): 
• 
A consequential modelling framework was applied implying the use of mar-
ginal instead of average data. 
• 
In case no subdivision was possible, system expansion was applied to handle 
multifunctional processes. 
• 
Background processes were modelled with marginal data. 
The above-mentioned modelling implications had repercussions on the modelling of the elec-
tricity and heat mix as well as fertiliser substitution. The marginal electricity mix was calcu-
lated following the methodology of Muñoz and Weidema (2021). The method regards changes 
in the electricity provision in a recent period in the past and extrapolates these changes as 
marginal electricity supply into the future. For the provision of marginal heat, EASETECH’s 
marginal heat mix was used (DTU, 2021). For the substitution of N, P and K, fertiliser conse-
quential processes from Ecoinvent v3.8 were used. The exact processes and substitution ratios 
used can be viewed in Appendix F-4. When deploying processes from Ecoinvent generally 
consequential processes were imported into EASETECH in accordance with the modelling 
framework. 
For modelling the software EASETECH v3.4.1 was used. As much as possible, primary data 
was used, especially for modelling the foreground system. Whenever primary data was not 
available, data from literature research, the Ecoinvent v3.8 database or assumptions based on 
expert judgement were used to fill data gaps. Primary data was mainly obtained from VCS, a 
technology provider of SS pyrolysis plants, Thomsen  (2018) and Faragò et al. (2021). Second-
ary data came from literature, the Ecoinvent v3.8 database and the EASETECH v3.4.1 data-
base. The exact sources for each process can be viewed in Appendix F.  
For the Life-Cycle Impact Assessment (LCIA), the methodology EF 3.0 developed by EC-JRC 
and recommended by the ILCD was used (Sphera, 2022). EF 3.0 enables the modelling of 16 
==PAGE:44==
Materials and methods 
28 
 
impact categories. In this LCA, 5 impact categories were taken into consideration, which were 
expected to be most influenced by the three assessed SS treatment technologies: climate 
change (CC), particulate matter formation (PM), terrestrial eutrophication (TE), freshwater 
eutrophication (FE) and resource use, minerals and metals (RUM). Furthermore, the impact 
categories CC, TE, FE and RUM were also used by Faragò et al., (2021), who assessed environ-
mental impacts from wastewater treatment and therewith also SS treatment. PM was added 
in order to account for PM emissions, which especially occur during incineration processes as 
used in incineration and SS pyrolysis. 
For interpretation of the results in the above-mentioned impact categories, each category has 
its own so-called “reference compound”. The reference compound is indicated in the unit of 
an impact category. The effect of the emission of a specific compound impacting a category is 
normalised to this reference compound to make impacts of different compounds in one cate-
gory comparable. Thus, the emission of methane has for instance a global warming potential 
(GWP) of 21 CO2e at a 100 years-time horizon, while the GWP of its reference compound CO2 
is 1 due to its lower impact on climate change in comparison to methane (UNFCCC, 2021b). 
The small “e” stands hereby for “equivalents”. This principle applies to the other categories. 
3.5. 
Pertubation, Uncertainty and Scenario analysis 
In order to ensure the reliability of results from an LCA study, it needed to be investigated, 
which processes and parameters have a high influence on the results, and where in the process 
uncertainties occurred. In the present study this was done by a combined perturbation and 
uncertainty analysis. First, a perturbation analysis was conducted. Hereby, each parameter 
used in the study was increased with a percentage of 10 %. Subsequently, the sensitivity ratio 
(SR) was calculated as in equation (2) (Clavreul, Guyonnet and Christensen, 2012): 
 
𝑆𝑅=
∆𝑟𝑒𝑠𝑢𝑙𝑡
𝑖𝑛𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑎𝑙 𝑟𝑒𝑠𝑢𝑙𝑡
∆𝑝𝑎𝑟𝑎𝑚𝑒𝑡𝑒𝑟
𝑖𝑛𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑎𝑙 𝑝𝑎𝑟𝑎𝑚𝑒𝑡𝑒𝑟
 
 
     (2) 
The ∆ in the formula stands for difference. The SR shows how the result reacts to change in 
the input of a certain parameter. A SR of 1 for instance means that an increase of 10 % of only 
this one parameter leads to an increase of 10 % in the overall result in an impact category. A 
SR of 2 accounts for an increase of 20 % at 10 % rise in the input and so forth. According to 
==PAGE:45==
Materials and methods 
29 
 
Laurent (2020) parameters > 0.5 were found to be sensitive. As this led to 18 sensitive param-
eters from pyrolysis, a normalisation to the highest SR (𝑀𝑎𝑥 𝑆𝑅) was undertaken for pyrolysis 
only according to equation (3): 
 
𝑆𝑅𝑛𝑜𝑟𝑚= |
𝑆𝑅
|𝑀𝑎𝑥 𝑆𝑅| | 
                         
(3) 
 
 
Important parameters were appointed to be parameters with a 𝑆𝑅𝑛𝑜𝑟𝑚 > 0.6.  
The sensitivity analysis was coupled with an uncertainty analysis to point out important pa-
rameters – parameters, which are both sensitive and uncertain. The uncertainty analysis was 
conducted following the methodology outlined by Weidema and Wesnæs (1996). Weidema 
and Wesnæs (1996) defined 5 data quality indicators (DQIs) together with a scheme to score 
their quality with regards to uncertainty – the Pedigree matrix (view Appendix H). The DQIs 
are reliability (R), completeness (CO), temporal correlation (TiC), geographical correlation 
(GC) and technological correlation (TeC).  
Reliability evaluates how trustworthy data is by regarding data verification and documenta-
tion. Completeness assesses missing flows in the available dataset. Temporal correlation ac-
counts for the gap between the time when data was obtained and the time when it is applied 
– in this case the time of conducting the present study. Finally, geographical correlation com-
pares, how well data obtained matches the study area, while technological correlation regards, 
how well the technology for which data is available aligns with the technology used in the 
study. After scoring the DQIs according to the Pedigree matrix a final data quality rating 
(DQR) is calculated under deployment of equation (4): 
 
𝐷𝑄𝑅= 𝑅+ 𝐶𝑂+ 𝑇𝑖𝐶+ 𝐺𝐶+ 𝑇𝑒𝐶+ 𝑋𝑤∙4
𝑖+ 4
 
(4) 
With R = reliability, CO = completeness, TiC = temporal correlation, GC = geographical correlation, TeC = technical corre-
lation, Xw = poorest score, i = number of applicable DQIs

After its calculation the DQR is subsequently rated according to Table 4.

DQR 
Data quality 
≤ 1.6 
High quality

Basic quality

Data estimate 
The results of uncertainty and sensitivity analysis were listed next to each other to identify so 
called “important parameters” – parameters being both sensitive and uncertain. Taking into 
account the outcome of the combined sensitivity and uncertainty analysis, a scenario analysis 
on important parameters was conducted. Thereby, important parameters were varied and 
their influence on the outcome was evaluated. Furthermore, the influence on the environmen-
tal impacts of different TDSS compositions in the input and different wastewater treatments 
in the pyrolysis process were assessed. Thus, the influence of using average and industrial 
wastewater treatment processes from the Ecoinvent and ELCD database and a wastewater 
treatment process based on own calculations was assessed (EC-JRC, 2003; Ecoinvent, 2021).  
The effect of different TDSS compositions was mainly investigated by varying the TS content 
in the incoming TDSS and scaling the other components to the adjusted TS content. Søndersø’s 
TDSS composition was modelled with a TS content of 28.2 %, 24 %, 23 % and 22 %. This was 
done as a particular focus of this thesis lies on the question regarding how the environmental 
performance of pyrolysis changes with a different energy content in the incoming TDSS. The 
TS content of 28.2 % was used as it is the TS content, which the technology provider used in 
their energy balance. Hence, results were comparable. 22 to 24 % are the expected TS contents 
in the input of the full-scale pyrolysis plant taken into operation at Søndersø.  
 
 
==PAGE:47==
Results and discussion 
31 
 
4. Results and discussion 
In this Section, the results of the LCI, LCIA, sensitivity, uncertainty and scenario analyses are 
presented and discussed. All results in Sections 4.1, 4.2, 4.3 and 4.4 were based on an input of 
ton TDSS from Søndersø with a TS content of 24 %, in the following referred to as “baseline 
scenario”. In Sections 4.1 and 4.2, the mass- and energy of the baseline scenario for the three 
considered sludge treatment options are analysed. In Section 4.3, the LCIA results for the three 
SS treatment technologies in the 5 selected impact categories are displayed and evaluated. The 
main results of sensitivity and uncertainty analysis can be viewed in Section 4.4, while find-
ings of the subsequent scenario analysis are debated in Section 4.5. In the baseline scenario of 
the three SS treatment technologies TDSS with a TS content of 24 % was used an input. 
4.1. 
Mass and nutrient analysis 
In this Section, the mass flow of TDSS as well as the flow of selected nutrient fractions through 
the three SS treatment technologies is followed. The exact fate of other TDSS fractions can be 
viewed in Appendix F-1.Figure 5 displays the mass flow of TDSS during direct field applica-
tion. In the baseline scenario, 5 % of TDSS is emitted to air during TDSS storage. Thus, from 
the input of one ton TDSS (FU), 995 kg are applied on the field leading there to emissions to 
air, groundwater and surface water, which are not shown in this mass balance, but included 
in the LCIA.

While the full amount of the initial K in the input TDSS is taken up by the plants, only 79 % 
of the initial P and 45 % of the initial N are taken up by the plants, respectively. The amount 
of N emitted to air is emitted in form of ammonia and nitrous oxides. 2 % of P is leached out 
into the water. These compounds have a high influence on impacts of direct application on 
the environment as further described in Section 4.3.  
A simplified mass balance for incineration can be viewed in Figure 6. As the model from 
Faragò et al. (2021) contained a combined drying and incineration module, emissions from 
incineration and drying to air are not distinguished, but shown together in one process. In the 
baseline scenario 91 % of the incoming ton TDSS is emitted to air, while 9 % goes to landfill.

The fate of C and nutrient fractions from incineration was not investigated into detail, as ac-
cording to Faragò et al. (2021) between 99 – 100 % of  the nutrients ended up in the bottom ash 
and were therewith landfilled, while 100 % of the initial C was emitted to air. The exact frac-
tions can be found in Appendix F-1.

In Figure 7, the mass flow of one ton TDSS in the baseline scenario through a simplified py-
rolysis process is depicted. 76 % of TDSS is water, which is completely evaporated into the air 
in the steam dryer. The difference in the graph, where 76.3 % of water leaves the system comes 
from water, which is recycled back as steam. With a TS content of 24 % 124 kg BC and 113 kg 
syngas are produced during pyrolysis. 15 kg LPG need to be added specifically into the sys-
tem to provide enough energy for the process. 1.6 l water per kg flue gas are used during flue 
gas treatment to reduce emissions from the flue gas in order to meet its threshold values. This 
is not shown in the graph. Finally, 946 kg flue gas leave the system. For more detailed infor-
mation consult Appendix J-1.

In Table 6, the fate of C and nutrients throughout pyrolysis can be viewed. Regarding C and 
N, it is visible that finally 40 % of the initial C and 27 % of N are sequestered into the soil. 
There is no uptake into plants visible because no uptake of C and N was modelled, as ex-
plained in Section 3.3.3. Scrutinizing K and P, it becomes apparent that only 24 % of the initial 
K and 44 % of the initial P is taken up by the plants. This means that, compared to direct 
application, roughly three times less K and two times less P is taken up into the plants from 
BC application. For K this is due to the fact that, according to mass balance data from Thomsen 
(2018), 76 % K is already lost to syngas during pyrolysis. For P, the main reason for the low 
uptake is that according to Raave et al. (2020), only 50 % of P applied on the field is taken up 
by plants and the rest stays in the BC. As Raave et al. (2020) only conducted a short-term ex-
periment the ratio of P taken up by plants could also be higher and is therefore further inves-
tigated in Section 4.5.3.

4.2. 
Energy balance of pyrolysis 
The energy balance for the case in Søndersø was based on data obtained from a technology 
provider. The technology provider ran their own pilot pyrolysis plant and provided an energy 
balance of a full-scale 350-kW pyrolysis plant based on experience from this pilot plant. In the 
original energy balance, the following points required a critical evaluation: amount of heat 
from the evaporated water, which can be provided to the local grid, energy of the outflowing 
gas, efficiency of the heat exchanger providing energy to the steam dryer and contribution to 
the energy balance by a screw conveyor (rotating helical screw blade within a tube for trans-
porting liquid and granular materials (Ningbo Precision Machinery Co. LTD, 2016)). Figure 8 
illustrates a simplified energy balance of the technology provider.  
 
Figure 8: Scaled energy balance of a technology provider of steam dryer and connected heat exchanger with an assumed heat 
exchanger efficiency of 90 % and an input of one ton TDSS (FU) in MJ 
Colour code: blue: Steam energy flow; green: Flue gas energy flow; orange: Energy flows unconnected to a mass flow; light 
green: processes 
==PAGE:51==
Results and discussion 
35 
 
The energy balance of the technology provider was scaled to the input of one ton TDSS (FU) 
with an estimated heat exchanger efficiency of 90 % to allow an easier understanding of the 
above-mentioned discussion points. Figure 8 only illustrates the part of the system which is 
most affected by the above-mentioned discussion points. The SS entering the system was in 
the case of the technology provider ATDSS with a TS content of 28.2 % and an energy content 
of 13.75 MJ/kg TS. The first arguable point in the energy balance is the motor energy of the 
screw conveyor. The screw conveyor is used to push ATDSS forwards in the steam dryer unit. 
In the energy balance of the technology provider, this motor energy – 120 MJ - also contributes 
to provide heat to evaporate water in the steam dryer. The reasoning behind this is presuma-
bly that while pushing ATDSS forwards, friction is created and therewith heat, which is de-
ployed to heat up SS. Whether this heat should be counted into the energy balance, however, 
is arguable.  
Focussing on the crediting of heat, the technology provider did not give any assumption on 
how much heat can be provided to the local grid. Hence, it was assumed that heat correspond-
ing to a temperature loss of 20 K from the 70 °C warm condensed water could be provided to 
the local grid with a heat exchanger efficiency of 90 %. However, this relationship was not 
implemented into Figure 8. 
Another disputable point is the question whether it is possible to close the energy balance 
without additional energy from LPG. According to the technology provider, 0.017 kg evapo-
rated steam/kg TDSS input is recycled back into the heat exchanger. This provides 7 MJ to 
the system. The flue gas leaving the system has a temperature of 150 °C corresponding to an 
energy content of 162.4 kJ/kg and therewith 161 MJ leaving the system with the flue gas. Heat 
efficiencies of gas-liquid heat exchangers typically range between 85 to 90 % according to ex-
pert judgement (Hannoschöck, 2016). Hence, the assumed 90 % heat efficiency seem realistic. 
If all this is taken into consideration, 79 MJ per ton TDSS input are missing in the system. The 
energy balance could still be solved without additional energy, as the steam evaporated from 
the system contains 1,803 MJ and if a higher amount of energy from this steam is recycled 
back into the system eventually no additional LPG might be necessary.  
Because of these conclusions and because of the fact that no energy content of the incoming 
TDSS was available, the following was assumed for the present study: 
• 
The energy of the TDSS input was calculated based on the Schwanecke formula. Miss-
ing H and O contents were scaled according to an average sewage sludge composition 
==PAGE:52==
Results and discussion 
36 
 
based on the analysis of several sludges with a similar TS content as the ones regarded 
in this study (20 – 30 %). The calculation can be viewed in Appendix E. 
• 
An efficiency for the heat exchangers of 90 % was estimated. 
• 
As the temperature of the outflowing flue gas is 150 °C according to a technology pro-
vider, it was calculated to have an energy content of 162.4 kJ/kg flue gas.  
• 
Despite the possibility that more energy from steam was recycled back into the system, 
it was assumed that the missing energy necessary to balance the system originated 
from LPG with a LHV of 46 MJ/kg (Engineering ToolBox, 2003). 
• 
The energy of recycled steam was assumed to be negligible. 
Even though it is questionable as to whether the motor energy really contributes to providing 
heating energy for the pyrolysis process, it was adapted as input into the modelling of 
Søndersø‘s energy balance of the pyrolysis process. 
The simplified energy balance of the baseline scenario can be viewed in Figure 9. Compared 
to the energy balance provided by the technology provider, the incoming TDSS had a lower 
energy content per TS: 12.6 MJ/kg TS compared to 13.75 MJ/kg TS. Furthermore, the TS con-
tent itself in the baseline scenario was 24 % compared to 28.2 % in the scenario of the technol-
ogy provider. Therefore, more energy was needed to evaporate the water content in the TDSS 
input and thus more energy left the system with the steam in Figure 9. Hence, 705 MJ addi-
tional energy were needed corresponding to 15 kg LPG/ton TDSS input. The additional en-
ergy was included in the incoming flue gas. Less energy left the system with the outflowing 
flue gas as LPG had a higher energy density than syngas and hence, less flue gas was pro-
duced leaving the system with energy, which is henceforth lost (for full balance consult Ap-
pendix J-2). 
 
Figure 9: Energy balance of baseline scenario in MJ corresponding to one ton TDSS input (FU) 
Colour code: blue: Steam energy flow; dark green: Flue gas energy flow; orange: Energy flows unconnected to a mass flow; 
light green: processes 
==PAGE:53==
Results and discussion
