4.3. 
Life-Cycle Impact Assessment 
In this Section, the potential environmental impacts of the three sludge treatment options are 
discussed under the five selected impact categories: CC, PM, TE, FE and RUM. A full overview 
of the characterized results of all impact categories can be found in Appendix G. As in Section 
4.1 and 4.2, the baseline scenario is considered. Figure 10 displays the environmental impacts 
in the five impact categories. Generally, it needs to be remembered that Figure 10 only illus-
trates summed impacts of the processes considered. This means for instance that when re-
garding the process field application that there can be impacts from the emission of e.g. ni-
trous oxide as well as avoided impacts due to the substitution of fertiliser. However, the over-
all result of field application only shows one number - either a positive or a negative number. 
But there could still be impacts or avoided impacts, which cannot be seen as the result only 
shows the overall sum of impacts and avoided impacts. 
==PAGE:54==
Results and discussion 
38 
 
  
Figure 10: Characterised environmental impacts of the baseline scenario in the 5 elected impact categories CC, PM, TE, FE, 
RUM per ton TDSS input (FU) 
Inc: Process belongs to Incineration; Py: Process belongs to Pyrolysis; SS: sewage sludge  
The small “e” in “CO2e”, “Ne” etc. stands for “equivalents”. Each category has its own so-called “reference compound”. 
The effect of the emission of a specific compound impacting a category is normalised to this reference compound to make 
impacts of different compounds in one category comparable. Thus, the emission of methane has for instance a global warm-
ing potential (GWP) of 21 CO2e at a 100 years-time horizon, while the GWP of its reference compound CO2 is 1 due to its 
lower impact on climate change in comparison to methane (UNFCCC, 2021b). This principle applies to the other categories.

4.3.1. 
Climate Change 
CC impacts of pyrolysis are the lowest among the considered alternatives with 5 kg CO2e/FU. 
This is followed by incineration with 161 kg CO2e/FU and direct application with 621 kg 
CO2e/FU. Scrutinizing direct application, the largest contribution (77 %) can be attributed to 
emissions from application of TDSS on the field. Hereby, nitrous oxide formation on the field 
is solely responsible for CC impacts. Nitrous oxide is formed during field application because 
of oxygen-deficient conditions in the soil (Wang et al., 2021). Thus, anaerobic processes form-
ing nitrous oxides are presumably responsible for the high CC impacts during field applica-
tion. Emissions during TDSS storage contribute to 23 % of the overall impact. Here as well, 
anaerobic processes leading to methane and nitrous oxide are mainly responsible for CC im-
pacts. Methane is the largest contributor with 72 % of the impact followed by nitrous oxide 
with 28 %.  
When regarding incineration, close to 100 % of the impacts originate from drying and incin-
eration. Regarding this process closer only 25 % of the impacts can be attributed to the flue 
gas itself. 56 % of the impacts originate from chemicals used for treating the flue gas, while 
14 % is of the impact is caused by electricity required for the combined drying and incineration 
process. This shows that flue gas treatment has an important influence on the CC impacts 
from incineration. 
The largest impact in pyrolysis originates from the flue gas leaving the system accounting for 
36 % of CC impacts, followed by wastewater treatment of condensed water from the steam 
drier adding to 28 % of CC impacts and LPG contributing to 20 % of the impacts. The main 
impacts from flue gas originate from fossil C in TDSS and LPG, which is oxidised to CO2 
during syngas incineration and subsequently emitted to the atmosphere. Note that these im-
pacts are included under “flue gas treatment” even although they are not emitted due to the 
treatment of flue gas, but the mentioned incineration of syngas and LPG.  
The comparatively high impact from wastewater treatment can be explained by nitrous oxide 
emissions during its treatment. These emissions were modelled based on own calculations. 
As there is a high variation of CC impacts when deploying wastewater treatment processes 
from different databases, this matter is investigated closer in Section 4.5.1. 
The reason for the overall low CC impacts is that pyrolysis substitutes processes which would 
otherwise have led to CC change impacts. For the substitution of these processes, pyrolysis 
receives a so-called “credit”, which has exactly the same value as the impact which would 
==PAGE:56==
Results and discussion 
40 
 
have been caused by the substituted technology, but negative. This credit corresponds to the 
“avoided impact” introduced in Section 2.4. The processes leading to avoided impacts from 
pyrolysis are the provision of heat for district heating with 27 %, the substitution of fertiliser, 
responsible for 40 % and carbon sequestration accounting for 31 % of avoided CC impacts 
from pyrolysis. This shows that avoided impacts due to application of BC on fields have a 
high potential for the reduction of CC impacts.  
4.3.2. 
Particulate Matter 
In this category, incineration performs worse than pyrolysis and direct application. In com-
parison to incineration, direct application and pyrolysis both have a 25 % lower impact on PM 
formation. However, the results are very close to each other and it is therefore difficult to draw 
clear conclusions as to which technology performs better, when regarding PM formation. PM 
from direct application mainly originates from ammonia emitted to air from TDSS, which has 
been applied on fields. During pyrolysis and incineration PM is mainly formed in the incin-
eration process and subsequently emitted to air. The process contributing to the low impacts 
from pyrolysis is the substitution of fertiliser, which leads to avoided PM impacts from ferti-
liser production.  
However, this outcome needs to be treated with caution. The modelled flue gas treatment for 
pyrolysis was based on the assumption that exactly the emission threshold values of the Dan-
ish EPA could be met as neither primary nor literature data of a pyrolysis plant with a water 
scrubber was available. Thus, impacts from an operating plant could differ. Furthermore, the 
incineration model was a case-specific model with emissions depending on the wet weight 
rather than specific components in TDSS, which are responsible for PM emissions. Hence, 
TDSS with a higher amount of N could lead to higher NOx emissions, which would not be 
reflected by the model. The same applies to the pyrolysis model. Here, it would be useful for 
future works to adapt both models to the composition of input TDSS. 
4.3.3. 
Terrestrial Eutrophication 
This category is expressed in Nitrogen-equivalents (Ne) and impacts in this category also 
solely originate from nitrogen compounds. When regarding TE, pyrolysis performs best while 
field application and incineration perform worst. Incineration and direct field application 
have roughly a 4 times higher impact on TE than pyrolysis. However, as for PM, caution needs 
to be applied when regarding these results as N emissions are not component dependant in 
==PAGE:57==
Results and discussion 
41 
 
the incineration and pyrolysis model and pyrolysis emissions were based on the assumption 
that exactly threshold emission values for NOx are met.  
4.3.4. 
Freshwater Eutrophication 
When regarding FE, the highest impacts are caused from direct application. These are around 
17 times higher than impacts from incineration and 170 times higher than impacts from py-
rolysis. This is due P leaching from TDSS applied on the field. For BC used on field in the 
pyrolysis scenario no leaching was modelled following Thomsen (2018). This leads to the low-
est impact among the three scenarios, because of avoided impacts from the substitution of 
fertiliser production. However, Thomsen (2018) merely assumed that there was no P leaching 
from BC. The value was not based on literature. Thus, more investigation of this matter might 
yield higher P leaching fractions and hence also here more research on leaching fractions is 
needed to better assess this category. The impact from incineration originates mainly from 
upstream emissions of chemical production used during flue gas treatment. 
4.3.5. 
Resource use, minerals and metals   
In this category both direct application and pyrolysis display a negative value in Figure 10. 
This means that impacts from the production of P, K fertiliser in the case of pyrolysis and N, 
P, K fertiliser in the case of direct application were avoided due to nutrients taken up from BC 
and TDSS into the plants. The avoided impacts from direct application are around 7 times 
higher than avoided impacts from pyrolysis. This is because no N fertiliser was substituted 
by BC and fractions of P and K are volatilised into the syngas during the pyrolysis process 
and can therewith not substitute fertiliser (see Section 4.1). Furthermore, the fraction of P fer-
tiliser substituted by BC is with 50 % lower than the one for TDSS, which is according to 
Yoshida et al. (2018) 73 %. As the modelling framework for this study is 100 years and the 
uptake fractions based on literature only consider a timeframe of a few years, it is arguable 
that over a timeframe of 100 years finally all P is taken up by plants. However, the same pat-
tern is also applicable for the category FE, where higher P fractions could be leached out over 
time.  
Pyrolysis also displays an impact on resource use due to metals necessary to build the pyrol-
ysis plant and electricity consumption deployed to run the plant. Presumably electricity con-
sumption has an influence due to resource consumption used for building the facilities 
providing electricity. Still, the avoidance of impacts because of fertiliser substitution exceeds 
these impacts. Incineration shows the highest impact on RUM, as no impacts are avoided from 
==PAGE:58==
Results and discussion 
42 
 
fertiliser substitution. Emissions originate from the production of chemicals, which are 
needed for flue gas treatment of incineration.  
Table 7 summarises which sludge treatment performs best in comparison to the other assessed 
technologies depending on the impact category. Note that the best performance is indicated 
with a minus for the lowest impact whilst the worst performance is shown with a plus for the 
highest impact in the respective impact category. An “o” indicates that the technology neither 
had the highest nor the lowest impact in the respective category. As values in the category PM 
lie very close together for all three technologies, the two better performing technologies re-
ceived an “o” instead of a plus to indicate the close proximity of the results. To assess which 
technology has a better overall performance, the impact categories would need to be 
weighted. This was not done in this study as the goal was to make a first assessment and to 
show which parameters are important for the environmental performance of the sludge treat-
ment technologies and therewith to decide where to focus the search for better data in the next 
assessment. However, pyrolysis performs best in most of the regarded categories according 
to this first assessment. As there are data gaps considerably influencing the results of PM, TE 
and FE, interpretations on results in these impact categories should be treated with caution.

4.4. 
Sensitivity and Uncertainty analysis 
A coupled sensitivity and uncertainty analysis was conducted as explained in Section 3.5. The 
focus of this Section is on identifying the most important parameters whose influence will 
subsequently be assessed in Section 4.5. Table 8 shows the most important parameters of di-
rect application. Parameters coloured in yellow displayed a |SR| > 0.5 and are henceforth 
deemed “sensitive”, while the SR of parameters coloured in blue even exceeded a |SR| of 1. 
Therefore, these parameters are called “very sensitive” in this study. Next to the SR the DQR 
is stated. DQRs > 3 were coloured in yellow to indicate low data quality. SRs of other param-
eters and the DQIs deployed for obtaining the DQRs can be found in Appendix H.

Colour code: Sensitivity ratio: orange: SR > 0.5, blue: SR > 1;  
Uncertainty: yellow: Data estimate (indicates an uncertain parameter) 
Scrutinizing Table 8 leads to the conclusion that field application and especially the substitu-
tion ratios – namely Slu_Sub_N and Slu_Sub_P - are among the more important parameters. 
These parameters define how many kgs of N and P, respectively, are substituted per kg N or 
P in TDSS. This shows that the substitution of fertiliser has a high importance for the environ-
mental performance of the system. However, the parameters drawing most attention are 
Trans_NH3_air and Trans_P_gw, as both can be deemed to be very sensitive. Trans_P_gw is 
the parameter giving the percentage of P leached into waters. The SR of 2.2 indicates a high 
importance of Trans_P_gw for freshwater eutrophication. Trans_NH3_air indicates the per-
centage of how much NH3 is emitted to the air per kg N in TDSS applied on fields. Because of 
their high sensitivity, these parameters will be investigated further in Section 3.5. The DQRs 
of the displayed parameters indicate basic quality for all sensitive parameters. 
In Table 9: Most sensitive parameters of pyrolysis with corresponding SRs and DQRs, the 
most important parameters of pyrolysis are visible. As described in Section 3.5 there were 
many sensitive parameters for pyrolysis. Therefore, the parameters were normalised with the 
==PAGE:60==

highest SR in each of the five impact categories to identify the most important ones. Parame-
ters with a 𝑆𝑅𝑛𝑜𝑟𝑚 > 0.6 were deemed to be “sensitive”, while parameters with a 𝑆𝑅𝑛𝑜𝑟𝑚 > 0.9 
were decided to be “very sensitive”.

When scrutinizing Table 9, it becomes apparent that all parameters are sensitive in the impact 
category CC. This shows that there are various opportunities to optimise the system in terms 
of CC impacts. However, when defining parameters for the scenario analysis conducted in 
Section 4.5.3, it was attempted to find parameters which are not only sensitive in CC, but also 
in other categories. Subsequently, two very important parameters were found which exceeded 
a 𝑆𝑅𝑛𝑜𝑟𝑚 of 0.9 and a 𝑆𝑅𝑛𝑜𝑟𝑚 of 0.6 in at least one other category: Steam_WC_vap and 
BC_Sub_P. Steam_WC_vap accounts for the fraction of water in the steam dryer, which is 
evaporated and hence does not enter pyrolysis, while BC_Sub_P is the amount of P fertiliser 
substituted by P in BC. As these parameters are so important, they will be examined in Section 
4.5.3.  
 
 
==PAGE:61==
Results and discussion 
45 
 
Most of the other parameters - Py_C_fossil_vap, FGT_CO2_fos, BC_TOC_soil - are related to 
CO2 emissions from fossil carbon either from incineration or from carbon sequestration due 
to field application of BC. However, as they are solely influencing carbon emissions and there-
with impacts on CC, they are not investigated further. An interesting parameter is 
LPG_TS_24_mass, the parameter defining the amount of LPG used in the system. This param-
eter has, on the one hand, an impact on the environment due to the production of LPG influ-
encing especially CC and RUM. On the other hand, it brings fossil carbon into the system 
leading to an impact from fossil-based CO2. According to the scheme established this param-
eter is not “very sensitive” and will henceforth not be considered in the scenario analysis Sec-
tion 4.5.3. However, its influence will be partly assessed in Section 4.5.2, where different TS 
contents in the input TDSS and their influence on the environmental performance of SS py-
rolysis are investigated.  
When regarding the data quality of the parameters, it becomes apparent that all other param-
eters are of “basic quality”. This shows that data still should be improved in further studies. 
However, it reveals as well that there is no sensitive parameter, which shows a low data qual-
ity at the same time. For incineration the model of Faragò et al. (2021) was deployed. As the 
model was not developed by the conductor of this study, but simply adapted to the case mod-
elled in this study no coupled sensitivity and uncertainty analysis was conducted on incinera-
tion. The results of a sole sensitivity analysis can be viewed in Appendix H.  
4.5. 
Scenario analysis 
Based on the results in Sections 4.2, 4.3 and 4.4 different sensitive parameters and processes 
were identified. Different scenarios were developed incorporating these parameters and pro-
cesses and their effect on the LCIA results in comparison to the baseline scenario. Thus, in the 
following Sections the effect of using different wastewater processes, altering the TS content 
and varying important parameters on the environmental performance of the three sludge 
treatments is evaluated. 
4.5.1. 
Influence of different wastewater treatment processes on pyrolysis 
As mentioned in Section 4.3.1 the type of wastewater treatment process used in the pyrolysis 
model has a high influence on CC impacts, but also on FE impacts. The other categories are 
not or only to a small extend impacted by the choice of different wastewater treatment pro-
cesses and are therefore not further investigated in this Section. However, characterised re-
sults can be viewed in Appendix I-2.  
==PAGE:62==
Results and discussion 
46 
 
Table 10 shows the results of modelling pyrolysis under deployment of five different 
wastewater scenarios. The “baseline scenario” is the baseline scenario from the previous Sec-
tions, where emissions from treating condensed wastewater were based on own calculations. 
Emissions from flue gas treatment were based on Ecoinvent’s average wastewater treatment 
process. The scenario ”Ecoinvent average” only used Ecoinvent’s average wastewater treat-
ment process both for treatment of condensed wastewater from the steam drier and flue gas 
treatment. The same principle applies to the other alternatives “Ecoinvent ammonium indus-
trial”, “ELCD average” and “ELCD industrial”.

CC: Climate Change; FE: Freshwater Eutrophication 
The process “Ecoinvent ammonium average” is a process for the treatment of wastewater 
from ammonium paratungstate – highly contaminated wastewater. The N-NH4+ content was 
similar to the N content of the evaporated water from the steam dryer and was therefore used 
in the analysis. Moreover, wastewater from flue gas treatment may be highly contaminated 
according to expert judgement (T Hulgaard (2021), personal communication, 29 November). 
Thus, the choice of using an industrial wastewater treatment process to model wastewater 
emissions could be justified. However, generally, most of Ecoinvent’s industrial wastewater 
processes have a similar CC impact as the presented average process and lie at most a factor 
10 higher than the average process used in the present study (Ecoinvent, 2021). Nevertheless, 
the impact of using different wastewater processes should be assessed more thoroughly in 
further studies.  The intention with Table 10 is hereby to point out the high range of possible 
results, which pyrolysis can have depending on the wastewater process used for modelling. 
As visible overall results of pyrolysis may vary over 2 orders of magnitude for CC and 4 orders 
of magnitude for FE impacts solely from changing the wastewater treatment process used.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:63==
Results and discussion 
47 
 
4.5.2. 
Influence of different TS contents on environmental performance 
Different TS contents may have an influence on the environmental performance of the differ-
ent sludge treatments. As became apparent in Section 4.2 the higher the energy content in the 
incoming TDSS the less LPG is needed to run the pyrolysis process, which can presumably 
improve the environmental performance. In this Section, the influence of different TS contents 
on the environmental performance was solely assessed by varying the TS content of 
Søndersø‘s TDSS input. The components in the input TDSS were scaled according to the al-
tered TS content. This means that for this Section it was presumed that by changing the TS 
content, TS fractions like C, N, P etc. were altered in the same way as the TS content was. 
However, this could be different in reality. Hence, an elevated TS content might not lead to 
an increased energy content, if energy rich fractions are not increased in the same way as the 
TS content. In the Appendix a theoretical case can be found, where not only the TS content 
was varied, but the whole composition of the input (consult Appendix J for more information).  
Table 11 displays the effect of a decrease in TS content of 1 % (from 24 to 23 %) on the envi-
ronmental performance of the three sludge treatments. The graphs and tables supporting the 
information provided in Table 11 can be found in Appendix J-3. The most significant change 
can be found when regarding the change in CC impacts from pyrolysis, which increase 63 % 
at a TS decrease of 1 %. Whereas the environmental impacts of direct application and incin-
eration fall with 4 % and 1 %, respectively, at a 1 % drop of the TS content.  
Table 11: Effect of 1 % decrease in TS content in input TDSS on the impact in CC, PM, TE, FE and RUM 
 
CC 
PM 
TE 
FE

application

-4 % 
-4 %

4 %

-1 % 
0 % 
0 % 
0 %

CC: Climate change; PM: Particulate matter; TE: Terrestrial Eutrophication; FE: Freshwater Eutrophication; RUM: Re-
source Use, Minerals and Metals 
+: increased impact 
-: decreased impact 
 
==PAGE:64==
Results and discussion 
48 
 
The reason for the high importance of the TS content on CC impacts is that TDSS with a lower 
TS content contains less energy. Therefore, more energy in form of LPG needs to be added to 
the pyrolysis system in order to run the plant. This leads to higher CC impacts at lower TS 
contents. As identified in Section 4.4 the amount of LPG added into the system is a sensitive 
parameter for CC. Therefore, a higher amount of LPG in the system has these large conse-
quences. 
When regarding direct application, a lower TS content also means lower amounts of C and N 
in the input. From this follows that less C and N is available to form nitrous oxide, methane 
and carbon dioxide. Hence, CC impacts are lower. For incineration lower amounts of C in the 
input lead to lower amounts of CO2 in the flue gas emitted to air and less chemicals necessary 
for flue gas treatment. It also leads to less heat produced, which can be transferred to the local 
grit and cause an avoided impact there. But apparently the effect of this avoided impact is not 
sufficient to counterbalance the lower impact from less CO2 in the flue gas at a decreased TS 
in the input TDSS. 
Looking at FE and RUM, a fall in TS content yields a surge in impacts from pyrolysis in both 
categories, while it leads to a drop in FE related impacts and a rise in RUM impacts from direct 
application. The reason for the rise in RUM impacts is that lower TS contents bring lower 
amounts of P, N, K, which subsequently end up in TDSS and BC, respectively, when applied 
on the field. Here, they can replace less fertiliser and therewith less RUM related impacts are 
avoided. The same explanation applies to decreased PM and TE-related impacts from direct 
application. Less N on the field yields lower ammonia emissions, which having a decreased 
impact on PM and TE. 
However, lower amounts of P in TDSS results in decreased P leaching from direct application 
– thus, the lowered impact in this category. As no P emissions from BC produced from pyrol-
ysis were assumed in this study, smaller P contents in TDSS and subsequently in BC only 
yield a lower substitution potential of conventional fertiliser. The expression “substitution 
potential” in this thesis refers to the environmental impact, which can be avoided through 
substitution. Thus, a lower substitution potential leads to decreased avoided P impacts, which 
would have occurred during production and application of conventional fertiliser.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:65==
Results and discussion 
49 
 
When regarding pyrolysis and the impact categories PM and TE a drop in the TS content of 
the incoming TDSS follows a fall in PM and TE impacts. This seems surprising as the other 
fractions in TDSS were scaled to the TS content. So, a lower TS content ought to lead to lower 
N and S contents in the input TDSS. This should subsequently to lower PM and TE related 
emissions – like PM, NOx and SOx - in the flue gas emitted to the environment.  
This is also reflected in the model. However, at the same time a drop in TS content requires 
more LPG and allows less fertiliser to be substituted as less P is contained in the BC. Emissions 
during the production of LPG and fertiliser are according to the model so high that they ex-
ceed the higher emissions from the flue gas in the pyrolysis process itself. Therewith a lower 
TS content increases PM and TE emissions. It needs to be acknowledged though that the 
model does not accurately reflect the increase in PM, NOx and SOx emissions as the emissions 
modelled did not depend on the amount of N and S in the TDSS input, but only on the amount 
of flue gas itself. This should be improved in further studies.  
Impacts from incineration stay constant. Generally, Table 11 reveals that impacts on incinera-
tion in the five impact categories are not affected by the TS content except for CC. The reason 
for this is presumably that the modelling of CC emissions in the model of Faragò et al. (2021) 
is based on the C content in the input TDSS. Thus, an altered C content due to a different TS 
content changes CC impacts, as well. That impacts in the other categories do not change can 
be attributed to the fact that the model was developed for a specific case and was not specifi-
cally designed to being adaptable to other TDSS compositions. Therefore, impacts in these 
impact categories might be affected by different TS contents, but the model cannot show this 
as it has not been designed to adapt for these changes. 
4.5.3. 
Important parameter variation 
The parameters identified in Section 4.4 as most important are closer scrutinized in this Sec-
tion. These comprise Steam_WC_vap and BC_Sub_P for pyrolysis as well as Trans_NH3_air 
and Trans_P_gw for direct application. The effect of decreasing the mentioned parameters 
10 % can be viewed in Table 12. Steam_WC_vap showed the highest SRs in Section 4.4 and is 
thus assessed more thoroughly. 
 
 
==PAGE:66==
Results and discussion

Decreasing Steam_WC_vap and therewith the fraction of water, which is evaporated from the 
input TDSS in the steam drier yields impact surges in CC, PM, TE and FE. The reason for 
increases in PM, TE and FE is that less evaporated water in the steam drier means more water 
in pyrolysis and subsequently in the syn- as well as flue gas. The model does not account for 
the effect this additional water has on pyrolysis or the LHV of syngas and therewith the in-
cineration. This is a shortcoming of the developed model as more water in syngas will reduce 
its LHV and therewith increase the amount of LPG, which needs to be added to provide suf-
ficient energy for the process. This would increase the impact on the environment. 
However, in the model, the increase in impact originates from the fact that the additional wa-
ter will increase the amount of syn- and thus also flue gas. As PM and NOx emissions are 
coupled to the amount of flue gas, a rise in the amount of flue gas yields higher impacts in 
these categories. At least for PM and NOx emissions this does not seem realistic as these are 
dependent on the compounds in flue gas, rather than its total amount. Thus, the model needs 
to be improved in this case. The amount of wastewater produced from flue gas treatment 
impacting especially FE, but also CC is also in reality given per amount of flue gas. Thus, it 
seems realistic to assume the same for the model and therefore no adjustments need to be 
undertaken here. CC impacts are further increased as less evaporated water means that less 
heat can be delivered to district heating and therewith less impacts are avoided.  
A drop in BC_Sub_P – the amount of P fertiliser, which can be substituted per kg of P in BC – 
leads to increases in impacts in all categories. The reason for this is that if there is less fertiliser 
substitutable by P in BC, there are also less avoided impacts from fertiliser production. In the 
present study the question is whether BC_Sub_P should not be higher than it is in the current 
==PAGE:67==
Results and discussion 
51 
 
model. In the current model 50 % of P in BC can be taken up by plants according to Raave et 
al. (2020). If longer periods than the 60 days in Raave et al. (2020) are regarded, higher P frac-
tions eventually reaching 100 % uptake rate might be achieved. The effect of these higher P 
uptake fractions on the environmental impact was modelled and can be viewed in Appendix 
I-3. 
Trans_NH3_air is the ratio defining how much ammonia is formed per kg N applied on fields. 
Trans_NH3_air solely affects PM and TE. With a decrease in 10 % of Trans_NH3_air impacts 
in PM fall 23.1 %, while impacts in TE drop 15 %. The reason for this is that the amount of 
ammonia produced is practically the only factor influencing PM and TE impacts in the model. 
Therefore, a slight decrease in the fraction of N converted to ammonia on fields has quite a 
high influence on how impacts change by the decrease. 
Finally, the effect of decreasing the fraction of P leached from TDSS from the field to water 
(Trans_P_gw) was assessed. This fraction solely affects the impact category FE. A decrease in 
the leached fraction leads to a decrease in FE impacts. If the fraction was decreased to 0 even 
negative emissions from fertiliser substitution could be reached. This is only reported to illus-
trate the importance of this parameter and the question not, whether there is no P leaching 
from TDSS application, but rather whether P leaching from the application of BC does not 
occur, as well. Following Thomsen (2018), no emissions were assumed. However, this is an 
arguable assumption, which needs further investigation. 
 
 
==PAGE:68==
Discussion, limitations and recommendations for further works 
52 
 
5. Discussion, limitations and recommendations for further 
works 
In this Section, the results of this thesis are critically compared to results from other research. 
Based on the outcome and on limitations identified throughout the report, recommendations 
for further works established. In close connection to this, the discussion addresses challenges 
faced when conducting the study, in order to provide a better understanding of why further 
research in the modelling of environmental impacts from SS treatment is necessary. 
First, the results from this thesis shall be put into perspective by comparing the outcome of it 
to other works. Generally, it needs to be mentioned that the comparability of the studies to 
each other is limited. This is due to different system boundaries, impact methodologies, data, 
literature and models used to perform the LCA. The fact that different impact methodologies 
were used and that few studies focussed on more impact categories than CC, lead to the cir-
cumstance that only CC impacts could be compared for most studies. Thus, the comparison 
to other works will mainly focus on impacts on CC. 
It was found that the question regarding whether an assessment starts before or after dewater-
ing has a high influence on CC results, as the TS content is significantly increased after de-
watering. Hence, a study beginning its assessment with SS after dewatering introduces a sig-
nificantly higher total mass of TS into SS treatment than a study starting after dewatering. 
This leads to the fact that Thomsen (2018) - who modelled the same system as in this thesis 
but included dewatering in his assessment – introduced 47 kg TS into SS treatment, while 
240 kg were used as input in this report. As elaborated in Section 4.5.2, the TS content has a 
high influence on CC results especially from SS pyrolysis. 
Hence, to increase comparability between the investigated works and this thesis, CC results 
of this thesis were recalculated with TS masses from other works assuming that  6 % TS is lost 
during dewatering, as in Thomsen (2018). The results of these recalculated CC impacts com-
pared to results from other studies can be viewed in Table 13. Results were recalculated for 
TS contents of 2 and 5 %. Results from Barry et al. (2019) were compared to the already existing 
scenario of a TS content of 28.2 %, which can be viewed in Appendix J. All values were calcu-
lated per ton SS input. Furthermore, Table 13 includes information on TS content and type of 
SS used for the LCA study. 
==PAGE:69==
Discussion, limitations and recommendations for further works 
53

5.1. 
Direct application 
At first, results of direct application from other research shall be compared to results from this 
thesis. Thomsen (2018) solely compared the environmental performance of pyrolysis and di-
rect application by regarding CC impacts. It needs to be remembered that the report was not 
peer-reviewed, hence it has not been validated by another researcher. Thomsen (2018) in-
cluded dewatering and sludge stabilisation in his assessment and his input was anaerobically 
digested SS from Ejby Mølle, while the input for the present study was TDSS from Søndersø. 
As visible in Table 13, the CC impacts Thomsen (2018) obtained (125 kg CO2/ton SS input) 
were in the same range as results from in this study, when compared with the TS corrected 
results (121 kg CO2/ton SS input).  
As Thomsen (2018) included stabilisation and dewatering in his assessment, results could be 
expected to lie higher than in the present study. However, the conversion factor giving the 
ratio of how much N is converted to nitrous oxide during field application (2.1 %) was two 
==PAGE:70==
Discussion, limitations and recommendations for further works 
54 
 
times lower than the one deployed in the present thesis (4.1 %). The N-to-nitrous oxide con-
version factor, SS composition and dewatering probably counterbalance each other leading to 
a similar result from Thomsen (2018) as in the present report. 
Mayer, Bhandari and Gäth (2021) assessed CC impacts from direct application on the envi-
ronment, as well. They found an impact of -4.7 kg CO2/ton SS input from direct application, 
which stands in large contrast to the results obtained from this study, which displayed an 
impact of 48 kg CO2/ton SS input after adapting the TS mass. One reason could be that anaer-
obic digestion is included in SS treatment, which might have led to avoided emissions. An-
other reason is presumably that Mayer, Bhandari and Gäth (2021) utilised a different emission 
model for the emissions from SS applied on fields, which was based on a model developed by 
Hansen et al. (2006). This model was developed in 2006. Hence, it seems more realistic to use 
more recent data for modelling emissions from field application as done in this study. How-
ever, the question of how to model emissions from direct application also depending on fac-
tors like soil type, weather conditions, water holding capacity etc. could still be the focus of 
future work.   
5.2. 
Incineration 
CC impacts of incineration were investigated by Gievers, Loewen and Nelles (2021) and 
Mayer, Bhandari and Gäth (2021). The results of Mayer, Bhandari and Gäth (2021) showed an 
impact of close to 0 kg CO2e/ton SS input, while Gievers, Loewen and Nelles (2021) detected 
impacts of around 7 kg CO2e/ton SS input. The adapted results of the present study lay at 
around 13 kg CO2e/ton SS input for the input of Mayer, Bhandari and Gäth (2021) and at 
31 kg CO2e/ton SS when considering the input of Gievers, Loewen and Nelles (2021).  
The discrepancy towards the results from Mayer, Bhandari and Gäth (2021) could originate 
from the inclusion of anaerobic digestion in their assessment. Mayer, Bhandari and Gäth 
(2021) presumably achieve an avoided impact from anaerobic digestion, which is not included 
in the model of the present thesis. However, Gievers, Loewen and Nelles (2021), who utilised 
anaerobically digested SS still detected an around 4 times lower impact than in this study. 
This shows future works based on the present study ought to focus more in detail on the 
incineration model to model its environmental impact more accurately. Another point under-
lining this is the fact that avoided CC impacts from provision of district heating are lower than 
avoided impacts from pyrolysis in this study. However, as incineration utilised all energy 
==PAGE:71==
Discussion, limitations and recommendations for further works 
55 
 
available in the input SS energetically, there should be a higher avoided impact from incin-
eration than from pyrolysis, which could not be seen in the results presented in this thesis. 
5.3. 
Pyrolysis 
Regarding pyrolysis, Thomsen (2018) modelled the same pyrolysis system as the system in 
the present report, although he did not specifically focus on the energy balance of the system. 
When comparing the TS corrected results of this study (1 kg CO2e/ton SS input) with results 
from Thomsen (2018) (42 kg CO2e/ton SS input), it becomes apparent that results from Thom-
sen (2018) are much larger than results from the present thesis. The main impacts from pyrol-
ysis in the study of Thomsen (2018) originate from a process called “thermal treatment”.  
This process describes the impacts of steam dryer and pyrolysis and includes therewith emis-
sions from pyrolysis with the flue gas. From the information gathered from Thomsen (2018), 
it could not satisfactorily be explained as to why impacts from thermal treatment are higher 
than in this study. The most probable reason is that Thomsen (2018) assumed a higher content 
of fossil C in the input SS than in this study, where 15 % were assumed (Boldrin, Maresca and 
Astrup, 2021). This leads to higher CO2 emissions from flue gas emitted and lower avoided 
impacts from carbon sequestered during field application. Additionally, Thomsen (2018) did 
not include avoided impacts from heat provision, which would have further reduced his over-
all result. 
Other authors found different CC impacts, which are nevertheless in close proximity to results 
obtained in this study. The study by Mayer, Bhandari and Gäth (2021) resulted in an impact 
of 3 kg CO2e/ton SS input, which was higher than the TS corrected impact of this study (0.4 
kg CO2e/ton SS input). A reason for this could be the different system modelled by Mayer, 
Bhandari and Gäth (2021). On the one hand, they included dewatering and anaerobic diges-
tion in their modelling. On the other hand, BC from pyrolysis was incinerated and subse-
quently P recovered from the ashes. Therefore, it seems difficult to compare results from the 
present thesis and Mayer, Bhandari and Gäth (2021).  
Barry et al. (2019) deployed TDSS with a TS content of 28 %, receiving an overall CC impact 
of around -20 kg CO2e/ton TDSS input. This result is around 2 times lower than the result in 
this study, probably originating from a higher energy content in the TDSS input. Cao and 
Pawłowski (2013) and Gievers, Loewen and Nelles (2021) both used a SS input with a TS con-
tent of 5 %. While Cao and Pawłowski (2013) received a result of around 0 kg CO2e/ton SS, 
Gievers, Loewen and Nelles (2021) obtained around 20 kg CO2e/ton SS input. The result of 
==PAGE:72==
Discussion, limitations and recommendations for further works 
56 
 
Cao and Pawłowski (2013) is very close to the TS corrected result of this report of 
1 kg CO2e/ton SS input. An explanation for the 20 times higher results of be of Gievers, Loe-
wen and Nelles (2021) could be that their SS input was already anaerobically digested. As 
shown in Section 4.5.2 the energy content in the input SS generally has a high influence on the 
performance of the system, but especially on CC. Hence, differences in the results could also 
originate from different energy contents in the input TDSS. 
In the present study, the energy content was calculated under deployment of the Schwanecke 
formula. When testing the accuracy of the formula on SS compositions with known energy 
contents, the energy contents calculated using the Schwanecke formula showed deviations 
from the real value of 4 to 44 % (see Appendix E). Moreover, for the present study no H and 
O contents were available. Thus, they were estimated based on other SS compositions with a 
similar TS content as in this study (TS between 20 – 30 %) (see Appendix E).  This shows that 
there is a high uncertainty on the energy content of the input SS. Therefore, it could be bene-
ficial for a next study to have a measured value for the energy content of the input SS or to 
find a formula allowing to calculate the energy content of SS more accurately than the Schwan-
ecke formula. Thus, the energy balance could be solved more accurately and therewith the 
amount of LPG needed for the system could be better predicted. Another reason underlining 
this plea, is the fact that the amount of LPG added to the system was found to be a sensitive 
parameter for CC and RUM impacts.  
Regarding pyrolysis there are three processes, which especially need further investigation in 
future studies: 
• 
wastewater treatment, 
• 
flue gas treatment,  
• 
and field application. 
As pointed out in Section 4.5.1 the wastewater process used for modelling environmental im-
pacts of condensed water from the steam dryer and water used in flue gas treatment has a 
high influence on CC- and FE-related impacts. Thus, it should be assessed in further works. 
Furthermore, the stabilisation of SS could be included in another study. As described in Sec-
tion 2.1, SS needs to be stabilised prior to its application on fields. Stabilisation was not con-
sidered in the present study, as it was outside of the system boundaries defined. 
==PAGE:73==
Discussion, limitations and recommendations for further works 
57 
 
Pyrolysis and incineration already inherit the stabilisation of SS in the process itself as patho-
gens are eliminated due to the high temperatures during the process, whereas direct applica-
tion requires an average sludge retention time of two weeks to reach stabilisation. This means 
that the sludge retention time could be shorter for pyrolysis, which leads to higher C contents 
of SS and a lower energy demand during wastewater treatment (P H Nielsen and N Almind-
Jørgensen, personal communication, 13 Oktober). Higher C contents in SS could bring more 
energy into pyrolysis, therewith improve the energy balance and reduce the amount of LPG 
necessary to balance the system. Therewith, the environmental performance of pyrolysis and 
incineration could be better in comparison to direct application if stabilisation was taken into 
account. This could be assessed in future works. 
The modelling of flue gas emissions affecting the impact categories PM and TE was based on 
the assumption that exactly the threshold values for flue gas of incineration plants - set by the 
Danish EPA - can be met. This was done as no primary data was available. Here, primary data 
is required to assess which impacts are caused by the emitted flue gas. Furthermore, based on 
this data, it could be evaluated whether it is in fact possible to meet emission threshold values 
of flue gas with the given set-up or whether additional treatment steps are necessary. These 
might lead to additional impacts on the environment. Finally, the modelling of PM and TE-
related emissions ought to be based on N and S contents in the input TDSS as they are actually 
depending on them rather than the total amount of flue gas used in this study.  
One parameter which did not appear in the sensitivity analysis as sensitive but should still be 
closer scrutinized in a further assessment is the fraction of P leached from BC during field 
application. The reason that the parameter does not appear to be sensitive is that the fraction 
leached from BC was set to 0 % following Thomsen (2018). Thus, if the parameter is increased 
with 10 % as done in the sensitivity analysis no change in the impact will occur and the pa-
rameter will not appear to be sensitive even though it might be. The results from direct appli-
cation show that a small leaching fraction of 2 % can have a significant impact on FE. There-
fore, and in order to assure a fair assessment between direct application and pyrolysis, the 
fraction of P leached from BC should be better assessed in further works. Another reason is 
that Gievers, Loewen and Nelles (2021), who analysed FE impacts of pyrolysis found an FE 
impact of around 2 orders of magnitude higher (1E-2 kgPe/ton SS input) than the TS corrected 
result in the present study (1E-4 kgPe/ton SS input). 
==PAGE:74==
Discussion, limitations and recommendations for further works 
58 
 
5.4. 
Substitution 
The application of BC on fields is responsible for most of the avoided impacts of pyrolysis - 
around 70 % when CC impacts are regarded. This is due to the sequestration of C in the soil 
and the substitution of conventional P and K fertiliser. This shows that the substitution of 
fertiliser has a high influence on the environmental performance of SS pyrolysis. The present 
thesis did not specifically concentrate on the substitution of fertiliser. However, the substitu-
tion potential of BC should be addressed more thoroughly in future works.  
On the one hand, BC bears the potential to substitute P and K and eventually also N fertiliser. 
On the other hand, BC could be valuable due to its many soil enhancing properties, which 
were addressed in Section 2.3.2. It could therefore substitute other agricultural products like 
compost etc. Hence, an important consideration especially for a consequential study is, what 
BC actually substitutes when applied on the field. If SS pyrolysis replaces direct field applica-
tion as SS treatment the question needs to be asked, whether it is not rather SS, which is re-
placed by BC rather than fertiliser. Due to the high avoided impacts from fertiliser substitution 
this might be an important point to consider for future studies.  
Another consideration regarding the substitution potential of BC is upgrading BC to activated 
carbon (AC). Initially, the aim of the present thesis was to investigate the production of SS-
based AC (SSAC) as an alternative to BC. However, as data from the technology provider did 
not become available in time, the substitution potential was evaluated in a rough qualitative 
assessment in Appendix C-1. On the one hand, the intention was to make a simple assessment 
to evaluate whether it is generally possible that the substitution potential of SSAC is higher or 
lower than the substitution potential of BC. One the other hand, the goal was to identify fac-
tors, which cause the substitution potential of SSAC to be higher than the one of BC and there-
with make SSAC the more favourable substitute from an environmental perspective than BC.  
It was found that the substitution potential of AC was similar to the potential of BC. The gov-
erning factors were the recovery efficiency and the substitutability. Hereby, the recovery effi-
ciency states, how much substitutable product can be recovered from the pyrolysis – in this 
case nutrients or SSAC. The substitutability is a quality-related parameter indicating, how 
much material is required to achieve the same function as the substituted material. This is a 
special discussion point, which could be investigated in future studies, as the quality of AC is 
basically compared by the BET surface area of AC. However, it is questionable, whether this 
should be the only indicator for measuring quality and therewith substitutability of SSAC. As 
==PAGE:75==
Discussion, limitations and recommendations for further works 
59 
 
indicated in Appendix C, SSAC might be better suited for the adsorption of large organic 
molecules, heavy metal ions and other polar or charged molecules than conventional AC.   
5.5. 
Uncertainty 
Uncertainty in this study was assessed by qualitatively under deployment of the Pedigree 
matrix (Weidema and Wesnæs, 1996). As described in Section 4.4, sensitive parameters used 
in this assessment were mainly of basic data quality. On the one hand, this shows that data 
still needs to be improved for further assessments. On the other hand, obtaining better data is 
not always feasible. For the emission of nitrous oxide for instance, Larsen et al., (2018) found 
that 2.1 % of N applied on fields is converted to nitrous oxide, while Yoshida et al., (2018) 
determined a factor of 4.1 %. In this study it was assumed that 4.1 % N was converted to ni-
trous oxide. This has a huge influence on CC impacts, also mirrored by the fact that the N-to-
nitrous oxide conversion factor is a sensitive parameter. 
This shows that there is a high uncertainty especially when modelling nitrous oxide emissions 
from field application. Moreover, Gibbons, Ramsden and Blake (2006) found a generally high 
uncertainty when modelling greenhouse gas emissions from fields. The generally high uncer-
tainty on the modelling of field emissions is one of the reasons, why the VUDP project men-
tioned in the preface of this study is carried out – to better assess emissions during field ap-
plication.  
A way to account for these large uncertainties would be to do a Monte-Carlo analysis. A 
Monte-Carlo analysis runs the model several times and each time chooses a random parame-
ter within a range of values found in literature or experiments finally obtaining a probability 
distribution and the most likely result of the model. However, to do this properly, parameter 
ranges of all parameters used in the model are necessary, which was not considered to be 
feasible for the present study. This could be considered in future studies. 
Regarding the uncertainty analysis, it needs to be remembered that when interpreting the re-
sults, there is a model uncertainty when calculating impacts from the life-cycle inventory. The 
factor on CC is 35 %. Hence, results of different scenarios are only significantly different if 
results show a difference of more than 35 % (Rosenbaum et al., 2017). This was the case for 
results evaluated in Section 4.3.1. For other parameters, no clear uncertainty values are de-
fined in literature, but uncertainties may well be around a factor 10 (Laurent, 2020). Utilising 
this factor would make results in the categories PM and TE insignificantly different and hence 
==PAGE:76==
Discussion, limitations and recommendations for further works 
60 
 
no conclusions could be drawn from these categories. Here, more general research is neces-
sary to assess uncertainty in these impact categories. 
5.6. 
Challenges 
In this Section, the most important challenges are described to provide an insight into the LCA 
process and into the question of why results in this study should be treated with caution. 
Perhaps the most important point to remember when examining the results of this thesis is 
that it is an initial assessment of the environmental impacts from direct application, incinera-
tion and pyrolysis. The model developed for direct application was mainly based on literature 
data with the challenge of sometimes contradicting data. A Monte-Carlo analysis could have 
allowed to take contradicting data into account and derive the most probable result. However, 
this requires time for finding parameter ranges for all parameters connected to an impact, 
which was not feasible in the given timeframe.  
Impacts from incineration were modelled utilising and adapting the incineration model of 
Faragò et al. (2021), which was designed to account for the impact of a specific WRRF. As 
mentioned in Section 4.3.2 the emissions in the model were based on the wet weight rather 
than specific fractions in the SS. Hence, its applicability to other SS compositions can be ques-
tioned.  
Most challenges were encountered with the pyrolysis model, which was the focus of the the-
sis. The model for pyrolysis was based on the mass and energy balance of two different py-
rolysis plants operated by VCS and a technology provider. Both plants were operated on a 
pilot-scale, while the goal of the study was to develop a model for a full-scale pyrolysis plant. 
The energy balance scaled to the input of a full-scale plant was provided by the technology 
provider. As the intention of the technology provider is to sell SS pyrolysis plants and there-
with to display results in a “positive light”, the energy balance provided had to be assessed 
critically. As the conductor of this study is not an expert in the field of chemical engineering, 
this was a challenging task. 
Another challenge was that no primary data was available for flue gas composition or flue gas 
treatment. Literature review yielded studies on pyrolysis plants and their respective flue gas 
treatment. But often essential information was missing, as for instance which norm cubic me-
ter definition was used. If information was found, it was usually available for a certain type 
of flue gas treatment including several treatment steps and therefore not comparable with the 
flue gas treatment in the present report. 
==PAGE:77==
Discussion, limitations and recommendations for further works 
61 
 
5.6.1. 
Recommendations for future works 
The intention with this Section is to summarise recommendations for future works identified 
in Sections 4 and 5. As the reasoning behind these recommendations are already explained in 
these Sections, they are simply listed in the following: 
General 
• 
Investigate the effect of stabilisation on the environmental performance of the three SS 
treatment technologies 
• 
Include other relevant impact categories in the LCA 
Field application 
• 
Investigate the fate of SS and BC fractions on field in dependency on different condi-
tions, such as soil type, water holding capacity etc. and its impact on the environment 
• 
Especially focus on the fate of P applied with BC on fields 
Incineration/Pyrolysis 
• 
Reassess heat crediting of incineration and pyrolysis as the pyrolysis model provides 
more heat for district heating than incineration, which does not seem rational 
• 
Integrate flue gas emissions in dependency of actual concentrations of relevant com-
ponents in the flue gas 
• 
Examine the energy content of the input SS closer and obtain an experimental value  
Pyrolysis 
• 
Implement primary data of a full-scale pyrolysis plant in the model, especially for flue 
gas treatment and heat provision for district heating 
• 
Closer assess emissions from wastewater treatment in dependency of components in 
the wastewater 
• 
Obtain better data for sensitive parameters, especially for the fraction of water evapo-
rated in steam dryer, the amount of P taken up by plants and the amount of LPG re-
quired in the system 
• 
Integrate a revised energy balance on the basis of the critical points identified in Sec-
tion 4.2 
 
 
==PAGE:78==
Discussion, limitations and recommendations for further works 
62 
 
Substitution 
• 
Reassess the substitution potential of BC with focus on nutrient fractions as this has a 
high influence on the avoided impacts from pyrolysis 
• 
Investigate the effect on the environmental performance of SS pyrolysis of substituting 
other products, such as compost or activated carbon 
• 
Closer scrutinize, what BC actually substitutes in a consequential study 
Uncertainty 
• 
Deploy Monte-Carlo analysis in assessment to include different values found in liter-
ature 
• 
Implement uncertainty on life-cycle impact categories as new evidence becomes avail-
able 
 
 
==PAGE:79==
Conclusions 
63 
 
6. Conclusions 
The present thesis was conducted with the main goal of performing an initial assessment of 
the environmental impacts of SS pyrolysis and the two most common SS treatment technolo-
gies in Denmark - direct field application and incineration. To achieve this goal, a mass and 
energy balance of the assessed SS treatment technologies was set up. The energy balance was 
implemented on the basis of data provided from a technology provider. This data was criti-
cally assessed and amended to the SS composition of Søndersø WRRF, which was the focus 
of this study.  
According to results of this study, pyrolysis performed best in most impact categories - CC, 
TE and FE - in comparison to the other SS treatment technologies. However, the superior per-
formance in the categories TE and FE needs to be treated with caution, as results in both were 
based on uncertain assumptions, which requires further investigation.  
CC impacts were mainly lower than impacts from the other technologies due to the seques-
tration of C in soil and the substitution of P fertiliser from pyrolysis. Direct application led to 
the highest CC impacts, due to emissions from nitrous oxide, when SS is applied on the field. 
CC impacts from incineration mainly originated from the treatment of flue gas. Direct appli-
cation performed best when regarding RUM-related impacts. This was because higher 
amounts of fertiliser could be substituted than from pyrolysis, while incineration increased to 
RUM impacts due to chemicals used during flue gas treatment.  
The study also regarded the effect of different TS contents in the input SS on the environmen-
tal performance of the SS treatment technologies. Lower TS contents had mainly a positive 
effect on direct application and incineration. On pyrolysis, lower TS contents had the reverse 
effect. They led to higher impacts in all categories regarded. This showed that the pyrolysis 
impacts can be reduced, when improving TS management.  
When comparing results of CC impacts in this study with other results in literature, it becomes 
apparent that results from SS pyrolysis were in line with results from other studies. CC im-
pacts from direct field application showed a large discrepancy to one result in literature, while 
it was in line with another result. This revealed the necessity of including a Monte-Carlo anal-
ysis in future assessments to accurately account for different emission factors from field ap-
plication found in literature. CC impacts from incineration were a factor 4 higher than a se-
lected result in literature. This indicates that a more thorough assessment of incineration 
might be needed than the one performed in this study. 
==PAGE:80==
Conclusions 
64 
 
The study showed that, when attempting to improve the pyrolysis model established in this 
study, focus should especially be on improving: 
• 
data on the energy balance to more accurately assess the amount of LPG required for 
in the system, 
• 
the wastewater treatment process utilised for modelling the treatment of water evap-
orated in the steam dryer and wastewater from flue gas treatment, 
• 
the modelling of flue gas emissions and flue gas treatment, 
• 
the water fraction, which is evaporated in the steam dryer,  
• 
data on field application, especially the substitution ratio of P fertiliser and the amount 
of P leached from biochar. 
This thesis ought to be seen as a first assessment of the environmental impacts of the SS treat-
ment options considered and results should therewith be treated with caution due to the high 
uncertainty connected to them. Another LCA should be conducted once data of the full-scale 
pyrolysis plant becomes available in order to verify conclusions made in this study.
