==PAGE:90==
Appendices 
 
I 
 
Appendices 
Appendix A Goal & Scope definition 
The goal and scope definition in this Section was created in the beginning of the study. Only 
the most important points were adapted in the course of the study. 
Appendix A-1. Goal definition 
Intended application of results 
The obtained results of the study are first and foremost used to compare the environmental 
performance of the three different SS treatment technologies with each other. In addition, the 
environmentally most impactful processes are identified in a contribution analysis.  Based on 
the identified hot spots different scenarios to improve the environmental impact are assessed. 
The results function as decision support to improve the impacts originating from SS pyrolysis. 
Moreover, the intention is to develop a first easily adaptable model, which can be improved 
as soon as more data becomes available.  
Limitations due to methodological choices 
When utilising the results of this study, cation needs to be applied when considering its gen-
eral applicability. The input data of the foreground system came from different water resource 
and recovery facilities with location in Denmark. As sludge composition and energy mix 
might vary in different locations, but especially in different countries the applicability of the 
results of this study to other locations should be assessed first. Furthermore, there are different 
pyrolysis technologies and depending on the technology deployed environmental impacts 
originating from these might vary significantly. Within the study it needs to be acknowledged 
that there might still be a discrepancy in the comparability of the environmental impacts of 
the different systems due to different SS compositions, emission factors etc. 
Reasons for carrying out the study and Decision Context 
Climate change is a topic of increasing importance. Until 2050 Denmark wants to reduce its 
net greenhouse gas emissions to zero (Danish Energy Agency, 2021). To achieve this goal the 
introduction of carbon negative technologies has become a requirement (Ministry for Climate, 
Energy and Supply, 2020). The pyrolysis of sludge could be one of these carbon negative tech-
nologies as it binds carbon in biochar for presumably 500 to 1000 years (Agriculture & Food 
LLC, 2018). However, this is not the only expectation to sludge pyrolysis. SS usually contains 
==PAGE:91==
Goal & Scope definition 
II 
 
heavy metals, organic pollutants and nutrients, which have adverse effects on the environ-
ment. The hope is that the pyrolysis of SS can reduce the emission of these substances and 
therewith their impact on the environment. Hence, this study is carried out to provide a com-
prehensive understanding of the impacts the new pyrolysis technology has on the environ-
ment. 
The influence of a study on decision making is affecting its modelling framework. Therefore, 
it is important to identify the decision context. As the present study forms the basis for the 
MUDP project – a known project assessing the environmental impacts of sludge treatment 
technologies in Denmark – it is assumed that the study is being able to influence large-scale 
decisions. This is because several WRRF operators might read the results of the study and act 
according to its results. Hence, the modelled scenario considers a roll-out of the pyrolysis 
technology of a technology provider over all of Denmark. This implies a decision context B, 
whose repercussions on the modelling framework are being assessed in Section 3.4. 
Target audience  
The target audience are decision makers from VCS, a technology provider and other Danish 
WRRF, which supposedly have a rudimentary understanding of LCA. Furthermore, research-
ers at DTU with and without a background in LCA are among the target audience. Hence, the 
study needs to provide enough detail and explain all technical terms from the “LCA world” 
in an understandable way, while still meeting the requirement of being concise. 
Disclosure to the public, commissioner of the LCA study and other influential actors 
The study is a comparative study, meaning that it compares the environmental impacts of 
different sludge treatment technologies. The report is intended to be disclosed to the public 
as it conducted at an open research institution and part of a project funded by the Danish EPA, 
which has an information responsibility to the public. The commissioner of the study and 
therewith main data provider is VCS. However, additional data might be provided by Aarhus 
Vand, a technology provider and BIOFOS. Hence, there are presumably opposing opinions 
on the outcome of the study, which the reader of this study shall be made aware of.  
Appendix A-2. Scope definition 
Deliverables, Function, Functional Unit and Reference Flows 
For each sludge treatment modelled a full Life Cycle Inventory (LCI) and Life Cycle Impact 
Assessment (LCIA) based on the life-cycle inventory is carried out. For a LCA it is important 
to ensure a fair comparison of the assessed systems or products. Thus, a basis common basis 
==PAGE:92==
Goal & Scope definition 
III 
 
for comparison needs to be established for each LCA – the functional unit (FU). To identify 
the functional unit at first the main function or main properties of the product or service pro-
vided needs to be identified. To figure out, what the main function of a product is main or 
obligatory and additional or positioning properties need to be distinguished. Table. A-1 
shows obligatory and positioning properties for the disposal of sludge, which is the common 
function of the different sludge treatment systems the LCA is build upon.  
Hereby are obligatory properties, properties a product must possess in order to fulfil the func-
tion it is bought for – e.g. a water bottle must be capable of storing water or a liquid for an 
infinite amount of time without allowing the water to leave it. Positioning properties in con-
trast are properties, which are “nice to have” – e.g. a certain water bottle can be light and since 
if someone would like to go on a hiking tour, he or she would rather buy this light bottle 
instead of a heavy one. But, if one is on the hiking tour and thirsty the heaviness of the bottle 
would not determine whether to buy a bottle at all, because it is “only” positioning, a nice to 
have property, but not obligatory. 
Table. A-1: Obligatory and positioning properties of sludge disposal 
Obligatory properties 
Positioning properties 
Legal requirements for disposal (e.g. heavy 
metal content etc.) 
Low impact on environment, esp. to Global 
Warming impact category 
Disposal of pollutant containing sludge 
Cheap technology 
Acceptance of partners 
Low investment costs 
 
Easy to implement 
 
Odour removal 
Based on the obligatory properties the functional unit of the system is defined as: “Disposal 
of one ton wet weight of dewatered & thickened sewage sludge of a plant specific composition 
in Denmark for a period of 100 years”. 
Since in this study not a product, but rather a service with the same input regarded, the refer-
ence flow can be identified as “one ton of dewatered and digested SS” for all disposal scenar-
ios. 
 
==PAGE:93==
Goal & Scope definition 
IV 
 
Life Cycle Inventory Framework 
The as situation B identified decision context has implications on the LCI modelling frame-
work. These are: 
• 
A consequential modelling framework is applied implying the use of marginal in-
stead of average data. 
• 
In case no subdivision is possible system expansion is applied to handle multifunc-
tional processes. 
• 
Background processes are modelled with marginal data. 
For modelling the software EASETECH is used. As far as possible primary data is be deployed 
for modelling, especially for modelling of the foreground system. Whenever primary data is 
not available data from literature research or the Ecoinvent v3.8 database is used to fill data 
gaps. Primary data will be obtained especially from Vandcenter Syd (VCS) and a technology 
provider. 
System Boundaries and Completeness Requirements 
The system boundaries for the different sludge treatments are displayed in Section 3.3. 
Completeness requirements 
Presumably for the pyrolysis process as well as direct land application. However, for the com-
parison between biochar, activated carbon and the fate of sewage sludge and biochar on the 
field primary data might either not be available or will need to be adapted to match the base-
line scenario. For a first iteration all processes are being considered and even in case no data 
is available they are going to be modelled under deployment of conservative estimates. In a 
second iteration data might be omitted after conduction of a first sensitivity analysis. 
Representativeness of LCI Data 
Primary data provided by the partners is fairly accurate in terms of geographical, technologi-
cal and time-related representativeness. However, as the pyrolysis plant might operate for 20 
years, time-related representativeness might not be met exactly. This is especially due to the 
fact that the energy mix is presumably going to change significantly in future. Even though 
there are national strategies and goals defined by the Danish government, which give an in-
dication as to how the energy mix is going to be altered in coming years, predictions about 
the future energy mix are still quite insecure. The same holds true for other inputs and out-
puts, such as chemicals etc. However, the change is expected to be most significant for the 
==PAGE:94==
Goal & Scope definition 
V 
 
energy sector. In the assessment an attempt will be made to adapt for time-related differences 
though presumably only in the energy sector. 
Additional data is withdrawn from the EASETECH and Ecoinvent database, as well as litera-
ture study. This data might not be as representative, especially when considering time-related 
representativeness, as some Ecoinvent processes are not up-to-date. However, the processes 
should be fairly technologically representative. Geographical representativeness is often not 
accurate, but this is strongly dependent on the deployed processes. 
Basis for Impact Assessment 
All impact categories taken into account. The methodology used for the LCIA is EF 3.0, which 
is endorsed by EU’s Joint Research Center (EC-JRC). It is recommended to be used by the EU 
and is therefore also being applied for this study as it fits the geographical scope of the study 
(Sphera, 2022). Furthermore, EF 3.0 was only recently updated and provides a comprehensive 
documentation of its methodology. All these arguments make it suitable above other possible 
methodologies.  
Only midpoints are considered in the present study.  No normalisation or weighting is ap-
plied. To enhance clarity only impact categories are going to be assessed, which are especially 
impacted by the subject of this study: SS management. To which impact categories this applies 
is assessed after the first iteration of the study. As the present study is a comparative study it 
was taken care of that the systems to be compared are assessed using the same methodology, 
system boundaries etc.  
Requirement for Comparative Studies and needs for Critical Review 
As the results of the study is intended to be publicly disclosed a critical review is required. 
The critical review shall ideally be undertaken one time alongside the study, when the results 
of the first iteration have been obtained. However, if this is not possible it will only be under-
taken on the final draft of the study. Furthermore, an evaluation of the uncertainty of the re-
sults will be undertaken.  
Planning and reporting of results 
As the study is a comparative study intended to be disclosed to the public the study needs to 
follow the ISO 14044 level 3 reporting guidelines. This is also intended. 
 
==PAGE:95==
Influential parameters for pyrolysis 
VI 
 
Appendix B 
Influential parameters for pyrolysis 
In this Section influential parameters and their consequences for the distribution of the main 
pyrolysis products, their quality and the distribution of pollutants is subject of investigation.  
Parameters influencing the outcome of pyrolysis are: residence time, heating rate, pressure, 
turbulence and SS characteristics (Djandja et al., 2020). Depending on the above mentioned 
parameters pyrolysis allows to shift yields between the three main products (Djandja et al., 
2020). The parameters examined in this study include temperature, residence time, heating 
rate and SS characteristics, which are among the most important parameters for SS pyrolysis 
according to Zhang et al. (2021). 
Appendix B-1. 
Temperature 
As already implied in Section 2.3 temperature is an important parameter for the pyrolysis 
process (Djandja et al., 2020). Zhang et al. (2021) have shown the effect of an increased temper-
ature on the yields of the three products. Table. B-1 shows that the char yield falls with in-
creasing temperature, while the gas yield rises. The py oil yield meanwhile increases up to a 
temperature of ca. 450 °C and decreases at temperatures above. 
 
Figure. B-1: Distribution λ of the three pyrolysis products char, py oil and syngas depending on temperature (Zhang et al., 
2021) 
This behaviour is attributed to secondary reactions of volatiles, such as thermal cracking by 
Fonts et al. (2012). Some of the newly created, shorter molecules form part of the syngas, which 
explains the steeper increase of the amount of syngas above 450 °C (Zhang et al., 2021).  
==PAGE:96==
Influential parameters for pyrolysis 
VII 
 
When regarding the syngas itself its composition displays a temperature dependency, as well. 
A rise in temperature leads to a drop in CO2 and a surge in H2. Concentrations of the other 
main components CH4 and hydrocarbons change, as well, however only to a small extend 
(Moško et al., 2020; Zhang et al., 2021).  
Zhang et al. (2021) and Moško et al. (2020) give contradicting information on how the CO con-
centration at lower temperatures up to 650 °C develops. Zhang et al. (2021) detected a slight 
decrease in the CO concentration, while Moško et al. (2020) found a steady increase. However, 
they both agree on an upsurge in CO concentration above 650 °C. Moško et al. (2020) views 
the rise in CO either as a result of the cracking of oxygenated compounds or the decomposi-
tion of oxygenated groups. These reactions form both CO and CO2, but because of the Bou-
douard reaction (equation (5)) the balance is shifted in favour of more CO at higher tempera-
tures.  
 
𝐶+ 𝐶𝑂2 ↔2 𝐶𝑂 
(5) 
The H2 concentration rises steadily as a result of the raised depolymerisation and decomposi-
tion of organic compounds at elevated temperatures (Zhang et al., 2021). However, H2 con-
centrations would according to Moško et al. (2020) increase even further, if it wasn’t for the 
reverse water–gas shift reaction (see, equation (6)), where CO2 and H2 react to CO and water 
vapour. This reaction takes place at temperatures above 600 °C. 
 
𝐶𝑂2 + 𝐻2 ↔𝐶𝑂+ 𝐻2𝑂 
(6) 
Temperature has an influence as well on the formation of NOx precursors like HCN and NH3 
in the syngas. The relationship is that an increased temperature leads to an elevated precursor 
formation (Djandja et al., 2020). As for the formation of SOx precursors Moško et al. (2020) and 
Tomasi Morgano et al. (2018) found a decline with surging temperature, while Djandja et al. 
(2020) detected the opposite. So, here literature does not allow a clear conclusion as to whether 
higher temperatures foster the formation of SOx precursors or not. 
When investigating other harmful components like PAH and heavy metals in syngas and py 
oil a similar pattern as for NOx precursors is drawn in literature. However, the pattern does 
not apply to all heavy metals. According to Zhang et al. (2021) heavy metal concentrations of 
Cd, Hg, As, Zn and Pb increase significantly at rising temperatures, while metals as Mn, Cr, 
==PAGE:97==
Influential parameters for pyrolysis 
VIII 
 
Cu and Ni mostly stay in the BC, also at higher temperatures. When looking at PAH concen-
trations in gas and py oil, these generally rise, when increasing temperature (Dai et al., 2014; 
Hu et al., 2020). In Table. B-1 the effect of a surge in pyrolysis temperature on the amount of 
those syngas constituents, where no contradicting information was found is summarized.  
Table. B-1: Effect of an increase in pyrolysis temperature on amount of syngas constituent in syngas 
Gas constituent 
CO 
H2 
PAH 
NOx  
precursor 
Heavy 
metals1 
Heavy 
metals2 
Effect of 
temperature 
- 
+ 
+ 
+ 
+ 
/ 
+: increased amount  
-: decreased amount 
/: no significant increase 
1: Cd, Hg, As, Zn and Pb 
2: Mn, Cr, Cu and Ni 
Bearing all the above-mentioned patterns in mind is important, when selecting the appropri-
ate temperature to run the pyrolysis in order to optimize it to meet the set goals. Another 
factor affected by temperature is the quality of BC. Table. B-2 displays the effect of an elevated 
pyrolysis temperature on parameters affecting the quality of BC according to Agrafioti et al. 
(2013). An upsurge in temperature leads therewith to an increase in total nitrogen content 
(TN), water holding capacity and CEC drop at an increasing temperature. However, pH, BET 
surface, available nutrients and heavy metal stability surge at rising temperatures.  
Table. B-2: Effect of an increase in pyrolysis temperature on parameter influencing the quality of BC 
BC parameter 
TN Water holding 
capacity 
CEC pH 
BET 
Available nu-
trients 
Heavy metal 
stability 
Reaction to 
temperature 
- 
- 
- 
+ 
+ 
+ 
+ 
+: increase 
-: decrease 
Thus, Agrafioti et al. (2013) argue that if the goal is to apply BC on agricultural land lower 
temperatures seem suitable, while higher ones fit with the goal of utilising BC as contami-
nant adsorbent.  
Finally, temperature affects the energy content of the three main products and therewith the 
energy balance of pyrolysis. According to Tomasi Morgano et al. (2018) an increase in temper-
ature leads to:  
==PAGE:98==
Influential parameters for pyrolysis 
IX 
 
• 
a higher energy content of syngas, 
• 
a slight rise in the energy content of py oil, 
• 
a drop in the energy content of BC.  
Hence, an increase in temperature leads to a higher amount of syngas and a higher energy 
content of the produced syngas. This finding can be useful, when attempting to optimize en-
ergy recovery from pyrolysis. 
Appendix B-2. 
Heating Rate 
The heating rate is another important parameter, which especially influences the amount of 
py oil producible. The heating rate describes the increment with which the temperature in-
creases in a certain timeframe and typically bears the unit °C/min. It can vary from only a few 
degrees per minute up to 650 °C/min (Djandja et al., 2020). A high heating rate maximises the 
amount of py oil, a low heating rate the fraction of BC obtained.  
According to Djandja et al. (2020) most studies show that the heating rate only affects the 
distribution of the main products up to a certain temperature – mostly 650 °C. This is 
attributed to the effect of temperature, which is the main factor influencing cracking reactions 
at higher temperatures, while the heating rate is responsible at lower temperatures. However, 
Djandja et al. (2020) reveals that there are also studies showing an effect of the heating rate at 
lower as well as higher temperatures. Hence, more research is needed regarding the relevance 
of the heating rate for the distribution of the main products in dependancy of temperature. 
Regarding the formation of of NOx and SOx precursors Djandja et al. (2020) provides the 
relationship of a high heating rate with a low residence time reducing NOx emissions, while 
the exact opposite applies to the reduction of SOx.  
Appendix B-3. 
Residence time 
Residence time is yet another parameter impacting the yield of the three main fractions. The 
relationship between residence time and increased NOx/SOx formation was already explained 
in the previous Section. When focussing on the py oil yield Fonts et al. (2012) states that the 
lower the residence time, the higher the py oil yield, as secondary reactions decomposing py 
oil need time to occur. This time is simply not available, if the residence time is below two 
seconds – the time, which is required to obtain a high py oil yield (Fonts et al., 2012). Processes 
providing these low residence times are fast, flash and ultra-pyrolysis (Djandja et al., 2020). 
Longer residence times lead to higher yields of syngas and BC due to the mentioned second-
ary cracking reactions (Djandja et al., 2020). Very long residence times up to days result in high 
==PAGE:99==
Influential parameters for pyrolysis 
X 
 
quality biochar as main product. This process is called carbonization. Slow pyrolysis is the 
process subject in this thesis. It has a residence time of 5 to 30 min and decreases therewith 
the py oil output to a minimum below 30 % (Zaman et al., 2017; Djandja et al., 2020).  
Appendix B-4. 
Sewage sludge characteristics 
Finally, the influence of the SS characteristics shall be described. One of the basic principles 
found in literature was that composition characteristics in SS were generally traceable 
throughout pyrolysis. Chanaka Udayanga et al. (2019) for instance mixed the same SS with 
organic humic acid and inorganic kaolin as well as CaO. Contents of volatile solids and C 
were lower in the inorganically adjusted sludges, while the ash content was higher. For ash 
and C the same pattern could be found in the BC: Higher amounts of C or ash, respectively, 
led to higher amounts of C or ash in the obtained BC. Whether the C content is higher is higher 
in syngas and py oil due to a higher C content in the input SS could not be answered from the 
literature review conducted for this study. 
Djandja et al. (2020) and Chanaka Udayanga et al. (2019) state furthermore that the VS content 
in SS is connected to the amount of syngas produced: The more VS in SS the higher the amount 
of syngas obtained. A similar relationship applies to BC yielding higher amounts with an el-
evated ash content in the input SS (Chanaka Udayanga et al., 2019).  
However, some inorganic species can also shift the amounts and constitutions of the main 
pyrolysis products due to catalytic activity.  Folgueras, Alonso and Díaz (2013) pyrolyzed SS 
with addition of FeCl3 and CaO and detected an influence from the resulting Fe2O3 and 
CaO/CaCO3 on H2 formation in pyrolysis gas. The amount H2 was increased due to the water-
gas shift reaction catalysed by CaO and Fe2O3. CaO and Fe2O3 combined lead to the oxidation 
of CO, NO, SO2 and HCl to CO2, NO2, SO3 and Cl2, while CaO/CaCO3 reduced SO2/SO3 
emission due to reaction to CaSO4.  
 
 
==PAGE:100==
Activated carbon 
XI 
 
Appendix C Activated carbon 
A rather new topic in research related to pyrolysis is the production of AC from SS (SSAC). 
AC is consisting of fused hexagonal rings, which are ordered in a disorganized and incom-
plete manner. This provides AC with a highly porous structure consisting of varying pore 
sizes, which chemicals can adsorb to (Betsholtz et al., 2018). AC can either be obtained by a 
one- or two-step pyrolysis. A two-step pyrolysis consists of a “normal” pyrolysis step, where 
BC is produced and a subsequent “activation” step enlarging the specific surface area of BC 
(Betsholtz et al., 2018). A one-step pyrolysis combines these two processes into one.  
The activation is either done chemically or physically. During physical activation, a pyrolyzed 
product like BC is exposed to temperatures between 800 to 1000 °C and water vapour or CO2. 
Vapour opens up inaccessible as well as new pores, while it enlarges existing pores. Moreover, 
functional groups are added to the surface as oxygen and hydrogen are bound to carbon at-
oms at the surface (Betsholtz et al., 2018).  
Chemical activation includes substances like NaOH, KOH, K2S, H2SO4, H3PO4, HNO3 and 
ZnCl2. It is usually a one-step pyrolysis at temperatures of around 300 – 800 °C. The activation 
mechanisms differ depending on the compound used for the activation. H3PO4, ZnCl2 and 
other acids remove hydrogen and oxygen from BC and enhance therewith its porosity. NaOH 
and KOH form intercalates, which improve porosity in the same manner as in physical acti-
vation once higher temperatures are reached. Furthermore, K vapours widen the distance be-
tween adjacent carbon sheets. In order to clean AC from the chemicals used in the activation 
process, several washing steps might be necessary (Betsholtz et al., 2018). 
According to Betsholtz et al. (2018) physical activation of BC from SS never yielded specifically 
high Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface areas. The BET surface area is the factor, which 
AC usually is evaluated against as a high BET surface area correlates according to Otowa, 
Tanibata and Itoh (1993) with an elevated adsorption capacity. Physically activated SSACs 
reached BET surface areas of 7 - 269 m²/g, while chemically ones achieved a maximum area 
of 26 – 1,686 m²/g (Betsholtz et al., 2018). According to Li et al. (2020), conventional ACs 
(CACs) generally display BET surface areas of 700 – 1,100 m²/g. Hence, some chemical SSACs 
reached BET surface areas of commercial ACs, while others stayed well below. 
One reason for the generally low BET surface areas of many produced SSACs is, according to 
Betsholtz et al. (2018), the high inorganic ash content in the sludge. The high surface area of 
==PAGE:101==
Activated carbon 
XII 
 
some SSACs produced under deployment of KOH and NaOH is attributed to the ability of 
KOH and NaOH to enlarge the BET surface area of the inorganic fraction.  
In adsorption studies performed, SSAC did not always perform worse than CAC. Betsholtz et 
al. (2018) states that SSAC is specifically adept to the adsorption of large organic molecules, 
such as dyes, heavy metal ions and other polar or charged molecules. This is because dyes are 
rather large molecules requiring large pores, which are more abundant in SSAC than CAC. 
Charged or polar components are rather adsorbed due to functional groups than their surface 
area and therefore SSAC might be better suited, as it generally possesses a higher amount of 
functional groups than CAC (Betsholtz et al., 2018). 
Appendix C-1. 
Substitution of activated carbon 
The original intend for the thesis was also to assess the environmental performance of pyrol-
ysis with the final product being activated carbon (AC) in addition to BC. As data from the 
technology provider did not become available in time, the substitution potential for AC in-
stead of BC is evaluated in a rough quantitative assessment in the following. Whether the 
substitution of AC instead of fertiliser is recommendable from an environmental perspective 
is depending on several factors: 
• 
the recovery efficiency of AC compared to P and K,  
• 
the environmental impact of the substituted material – conventional AC vs. conven-
tional P and K fertiliser, 
• 
the substitutability of conventional AC by SSAC in relation to the substitutability of P 
and K fertiliser by BC, 
• 
 and the market response towards SSAC and BC, respectively (Vadenbo, Hellweg and 
Astrup, 2017). 
To allow for an easier comparison solely the substitution potential of substituting P fertiliser 
with BC is regarded. If it is assumed that the same amount of AC can be produced from one 
ton of TDSS as from BC, then 124 kg AC and 5 kg P can be recovered from one ton TDSS. This 
means that roughly 25 times more AC is produced from one ton TDSS than pure P.  
To simplify the comparison of environmental impacts solely CC impacts are considered in 
this comparison. When regarding CC impacts from the production of P fertiliser, 1 kg P2O5 
fertiliser deployed in Denmark emits 3 kg CO2e (Ecoinvent, 2021), while the production of 
1 kg AC in Europe leads to 9 kg CO2e emitted. However, most of the impacts for the produc-
tion comes from heat and electricity of the production process. If it is assumed that heat and 
==PAGE:102==
Activated carbon 
XIII 
 
electricity required for producing SSAC is the same as for conventional AC, only the raw ma-
terial used for AC production can be substituted – 3 kg hard coal, having CC impacts of 1 
kg CO2e (Ecoinvent, 2021). Thus, per kg P 3 kg CO2e can be substituted, per kg SSAC 1 kg 
CO2e. 
When comparing the substitutability of P in BC to conventional P2O5 fertiliser, only 50 % of P 
were assumed to be taken up into the plants from BC, while the uptake from fertiliser was 
assumed to be 100 %. As the amount of P2O5 fertiliser is given per P2O5, but the amount of BC 
is given per pure P, more fertiliser can be substituted by P leading to roughly 1 kg P2O5 ferti-
liser per kg P.  
The substitutability of AC depends on the quality of SSAC. Typically, the quality of AC is 
assessed by the BET surface area. However, it is questionable whether this approach is the 
approach is generally applicable. As indicated in Section 2.3.2, SSAC might be better suited 
for the adsorption of large organic molecules, heavy metal ions and other polar or charged 
molecules. Furthermore, when assuming the BET surface area as sole indicator for the quality 
of AC, this indicates that the amount of AC needed to reach a certain adsorption is simply 
scalable to its BET surface area. But this is not certain and needs to be investigated more into 
detail in further studies. However, for this simple evaluation of the substitution potential of 
SSAC the assumption is that solely the BET surface area is influencing the quality and there-
with substitutability of AC.  
With physical activation, which is used by the technology provider BET surface areas of 7 - 
269 m²/g may be reached (Betsholtz et al., 2018). Conventional ACs have BET surface areas of 
700 – 1,100 m²/g (Li et al., 2020). From this information it was assumed that 10 times more 
SSAC was needed to reach the same adsorption as from conventional AC leading to a substi-
tutability of 0.1. The market response for both P from BC and SSAC was assumed to be 1, 
indicating no difference between conventional and the SS-based products.  
Based on the information above, Table. C-1 shows a rough comparison of the substitution 
potential of BC applied on fields compared to AC. AC substitution factors were normalised 
to with the substitution factor of BC.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:103==
Activated carbon 
XIV 
 
Table. C-1: Substitution potential of BC compared to SSAC normalised to BC

1

1

Table. C-1 gives the impression that the substitution potential of AC is lower than the substi-
tution potential of BC applied on fields. However, the key message from the table is rather 
what strongly influences the substitution potential of AC – the recovery efficiency of AC and 
its substitutability. If the substitutability of AC can be doubled for instance the substitution 
potential of AC is higher than from BC. Hence, this rough assessment shows that it can be 
useful to consider the production of AC instead of BC when attempting to improve the envi-
ronmental performance of pyrolysis. 
 
==PAGE:104==
 
 
XXIX 
 
Appendix D Marginal Electricity 
The marginal electricity mix was calculated based on Table. D-1 and Table. D-2. When regarding electricity the use of marginal data implies that 
instead of deploying the current Danish average electricity mix, it is attempted to assess, which electricity is actually going to be utilised or 
substituted, if there is a tiny or marginal in- or decrease in demand originating from a new technology.  
There are different ways of assessing this. In this study the marginal electricity mix is calculated according to Muñoz and Weidema, (2021). It is 
based on the change in electricity provision in the period from 2015 to 2020. Increasingly used forms of energies are assumed to be the future 
marginal electricity mix including imports from Germany. In the period assessed no increase in the demand from Sweden was visible. Therefore, 
Sweden was not assumed to be a future marginal supplier. This can, however, be criticised as there is also a variation in the electricity supply 
depending on the year and hence eventually in a timeframe beginning one year earlier Sweden could have also been a marginal supplier. 
To calculate the electricity mix, first the annual rate was calculated by comparing the amount of energy supplied by a marginal supplier in 2015 
and 2020. To assess, whether a technology is likely to be a marginal supplier in the future, it was assessed, whether a technology can be classified 
as “modern” or “old”. This was done by calculating the net annual growth 2015 – 2020. The net annual growth was the difference of annual 
growth and capital replacement and a technology was considered to be old, if it displayed a negative value and modern if the result was positive. 
The reasoning behind this is that if the capital replacement rate is larger than the annual growth rate, the supply is faster faced out than it grows. 
Thus, these suppliers are not likely to be a marginal supplier in the future. Only “modern” suppliers were assumed to contribute to the marginal 
electricity mix.  
A similar principle was assumed to calculate the electricity supply from Germany to Denmark. However, photovoltaic, wind and geothermal 
energy were not considered as marginal suppliers. This was done as photovoltaic and geothermal energy usually operate at low voltages and 
hence could not be assumed to supply energy to Denmark. As wind is a flexible energy source, which is has a high supply across neighbouring 
countries at the same time, it was not assumed to be exported from Germany to Denmark.

Calculation of energy content of Søndersø SS

Appendix E 
Calculation of energy content of Søndersø SS 
The energy content of TDSS from Søndersø was calculated with the Schwanecke formula introduced in Section 3.3.3. The TDSS composition was 
an average composition obtained from measurements over a 2 years timeframe in 2019/2020. The TOC content was solely available from one 
measurement (N. Almind-Jørgensen, personal communication, 2021) As no H and O contents were available from the measurements, which are 
needed when calculating the energy content with the Schwanecke, H and O contents were estimated from known SS compositions with a similar 
TS content as the one regarded in this study (20 – 30 %). SS compositions were obtained from the Phyllis database (TNO Biobased and Circular 
Technologies, 2022). The SS compositions from the database are displayed in Table. E-1. Furthermore, the average C/H and C/O ratio is shown 
as this was used to approximate and O contents in Søndersø‘s TDSS. Schwanecke-based calculated LHVs and their deviation from the real value 
are displayed, as well. Deviations ranged from 4 to 44 %.

Appendix I 
Scenario analysis

assumption that the ATDSS composition was the same as from Ejby Mølle in the year 2019 to 2020.
